(الثامن أن الأدعية المذكورة (الخ قال الشيخ علاء الدين الحصكفي في شرح التنوير قد رواه ابن حبان وغيره عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام من طرق . قال محقق الشافعية الرملي : فيعمل به في فضائل الأعمال وإن أنكره النووي .اهـ. قوله : وظاهره أنه بالكسر أعم اهـ قال الشيخ خير الدين الرملي : أقول فيه ،نظر فإن المراد ما هو متنجس كالثوب كما صرح به ابن ملك وحينئذ ضبطه بالفتح هو والفتح كما لا يخفى. والنقض في الجسم فك تأليفه. وفي غيره إخراجه عن إفادة ما المقصود منه كإستباحة الصلاة في الوضوء، . وأفاد بقوله «خروج نجس أن الناقض خروجه لا عينه وعلل له في الكافي بأن الخروج علة الانتقاض وهي عبارة عن المعنى، وعلل شراح الهداية بأنها لو كانت نفسها ناقضة لما حصلت طهارة لشخص أصلاً لأن تحت كل جلدة دماً لكن قال في فتح القدير: الظاهر أن الناقض النجس الخارج وبينه بما حاصله أن الناقض هو المؤثر للنقض والضد هو المؤثر في رفع ضده وصفة النجاسة الرافعة للطهارة إنما هي بالخارج فالعلة للنقض هي النجاسة بشرط الخروج، وتأيد هذا بظاهر الحديث ما الحدث؟ قال: ما يخرج من السبيلين. فالعلة النجاسة والخروج علة العلة وإضافة الحكم إلى العلة أولى من إضافته إلى علة العلة فاندفع بهذا ما قالوا من لزوم عدم حصول طهارة لشخص على تقدير إضافة النقض إلى النجاسة إذ لا يلزم إلا لو قلنا بأن الخروج ليس شرطاً في عمل العلة ولا علة العلة. وشمل كلامه جميع النواقض الحقيقية وهو مجمل وهو قسمان: خارج من السبيلين وخارج من غيرهما. فالأول ناقض مطلقاً فتنقض الدودة الخارجة من الدبر والذكر والفرج كذا في الخانية. وفي السراج : إنه بالإجماع. فما في التبيين من أن الدودة الخارجة من فرجها على الخلاف ففيه نظر، وعلل في البدائع بكون الدودة ناقضة أنها نجسة لتولدها من النجاسة، وذكر الاسبيجابي أن فيها طريقتين : إحداهما ما ذكرناه والثانية أن الناقض ما عليها واختاره الزيلعي وهو في الحصاة مسلم. ولا يرد على المصنف الريح الخارجة من الذكر وفرج المرأة فإنها لا تنقض الوضوء على الصحيح لأن الخارج منهما اختلاج وليس بريح خارجة . ولو سلم فلیست بمنبعثة عن محل النجاسة والريح لا ينقض إلا لذلك لا لأن عينها نجسة لأن الصحيح أن عينها طاهرة حتى لو لبس سروال مبتلة أو ابتل من أليتيه الموضع الذي يمر به الريح فخرج الريح لا يتنجس وهو قول العامة وما نقل عن الحلواني من أنه كان لا يصلي راويله فورع منه كذا قالوا فاندفع بهذا ما ذكره مسكين في شرحه من أن كلام المصنف ليس على عمومه كما لا يخفى ودخل أيضاً ما لو أدخل أصبعه في دبره ولم يغيبها فإنه تعتبر فيه البلة والرائحة وهو الصحيح لأنه ليس بداخل من كل وجه كذا في شرح قاضيخان . بسرا والخرج متعين، ويدخل فيه ما خرج متنجساً باعتبار خروج النجاسة التي به فصدق عليه خروج النجس فتأمل فإنه بالفتح أشمل والله تعالى أعلم. قوله : (وهي عبارة عن (المعنى أي والعلة عبارة عن المعنى كذلك هو معنى قوله : (ليس شرطاً في عمل العلة ولا علة العلة معطوف على قوله «ليس شرطاً». قوله : (لأن الصحيح أن عينها طاهرة قال الرملي : أقول فيشكل عليه بعدم دخول الخارجة من الدبر في كلامه إلا أن يقال: إنها وإن لم تكن عينها نجسة لكنها متنجسة فتدخل فيه، سواء قرى قوله «نجس» بالفتح أو بالكسر إذ لا فرق بينهما لغة فتأمل قوله : فلا يترتب عليه الخروج) وهذا
He performs wudu with the prescribed wudu. So in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. And you have learned from what we have presented that increasing beyond three [washes] for tranquility of heart or with the intention of another wudu is not objectionable, so what they have stated here should be restricted. Second: that refraining from speaking to people is not considered etiquette unless there is no need; if a need arises that one fears missing by abandoning speech, then speaking is not a lack of etiquette, as in Sharh al-Munya. Third: that preparing for wudu before the time is restricted to one who is not excused; and in Sharh al-Munya, I consider it from the etiquette of prayer, not wudu, because wudu is intended for performing prayer. Fourth: that al-Zayla'i explicitly stated that slapping the face with water is disliked, so abandoning it is a sunna, not an etiquette. Fifth: that his mention of dalk (rubbing) after mentioning passing the hand over the limbs is repetition, because dalk, as in Sharh al-Munya, is passing the wet hand over the washed limbs, and it should be added 'with leaning'. Sixth: that he mentioned dalk as recommended, but in al-Khulasa it is sunna according to us. Seventh: that he mentioned from them filling his vessel in preparation, and it should be restricted to when he does not take wudu from a river or basin, because taking wudu from them is easier than from a vessel. Eighth: that the supplications mentioned in fiqh books—al-Nawawi said: they have no basis; but what is confirmed is the testimony after completing wudu, and al-Siraj al-Hindi affirmed it in al-Tawshih. Ninth: that among them is washing under the eyebrows and mustache due to lack of difficulty. Tenth: that praying two rak'ahs after wudu is only recommended if it is not a disliked time. Eleventh: that among them is combining intention of heart with action of the tongue as in al-Mi'raj. Twelfth: that he should not perform wudu in impure places because water of wudu has sanctity, as in al-Mudmarat. Thirteenth: that among them is that he should begin washing the face from the top, and in wiping, and for the hand and foot, from the fingertips as in al-Mi'raj. Fourteenth: that among them is inserting his little finger into the ear canal. Fifteenth: that among them is sending blessings upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) for every limb as in al-Tabyin. He said: (And it is nullified by the exit of impurity from him) meaning wudu is nullified by the exit of impurity from the one performing wudu. Al-Najas (with two fatha) technically is the substance of impurity, and with kasra of the jim it is what is not pure. In language there is no difference between them as in Sharh al-Wiqaya, and it appears that with kasra it is more general, so recording it in al-Mukhtar with kasra is correct. The head with its front part... etc. refers back to extravagance. And his statement: 'what is in al-Muntaqa agrees with what is in al-Siraj' is correct as what is in al-Khaniyya, as is not hidden, since there is no mention of al-Siraj in his words nor in the words of the commentator. He said: (And the fifth, that his mention of dalk etc.) can be answered that his meaning is passing the wet hand over the washed limbs, as the commentator mentioned when discussing washing the face from Khalaf ibn Ayyub that he said: It is appropriate for the one performing wudu in winter to moisten his limbs with water like oil then let water flow over them because water repels from limbs in winter. End quote. But he should have restricted it to winter, reflect. He said: (The eighth, that the supplications mentioned etc.) said Shaykh Ala' al-Din al-Haskafi in Sharh al-Tanwir: It has been narrated by Ibn Hibban and others from him (peace be upon him) through multiple chains. The researcher of the Shafi'is, al-Ramli, said: It is acted upon in virtuous deeds even if al-Nawawi denied it. End quote. He said: And it appears that with kasra it is more general. End quote. Said Shaykh Khayr al-Din al-Ramli: I say there is consideration in it, because what is meant is what is contaminated like a garment, as stated by Ibn Malik, and then recording it with fatha is correct. It is with fatha and fatha as is not hidden. And nullification in a body is undoing its structure; in other things it is taking it out from providing what is intended from it, like permitting prayer with wudu. And he benefited with his statement 'exit of impurity' that the nullifier is its exit, not its substance. And it is explained in al-Kafi that exit is the cause of nullification, which is an expression of the meaning. And the commentators of al-Hidaya explained that if the substance itself were a nullifier, then a person would never attain purity at all because under every skin there is blood. But in Fath al-Qadir it is said: The apparent is that the nullifier is the impurity that exits, and he explained it with the gist that the nullifier is the effecter of nullification, and the opposite is the effecter of removal of its opposite, and the quality of impurity that removes purification is only with exit. So the cause for nullification is impurity with the condition of exit. And this is supported by the apparent of the hadith: 'What is hadath?' He said: 'What exits from the two passages.' So the cause is impurity, and exit is the cause of the cause. Attributing the ruling to the cause is better than attributing it to the cause of the cause. So what they said—about the necessary consequence that purification would not be attained for a person if nullification were attributed to impurity—is refuted by this, because it only follows if we say that exit is not a condition in the operation of the cause nor a cause of the cause. And his words include all true nullifiers, which is a general category. It is of two types: exiting from the two passages and exiting from elsewhere. The first is a nullifier absolutely; so a worm exiting from the anus, the male organ, and the vagina is a nullifier, as in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Siraj: it is by consensus. So what is in al-Tabyin that a worm exiting from the vulva is subject to disagreement is questionable. It is explained in al-Bada'i that the worm is a nullifier because it is impure since it originates from impurity. And al-Isbijabi mentioned there are two methods: one is what we mentioned, and the second is that the nullifier is what is upon it (i.e., the impurity on the worm), and al-Zayla'i chose this, and it is conceded regarding a pebble. And what is not objected against the author is wind exiting from the male organ and female vulva, for it does not nullify wudu, as the correct view, because what exits from them is a spasm and not wind exiting. Even if conceded, it is not emanating from the place of impurity; wind nullifies only for that reason, not because its substance is impure, because the correct view is that its substance is pure, such that if he wears a wet trouser or the area on his buttocks through which the wind passes becomes wet, and the wind exits, it does not become impure. And this is the saying of the general scholars. And what is transmitted from al-Halwani that he would not pray with his trousers is a scruple from him. They said so. So what Miskin mentioned in his commentary—that the author's words are not general—is refuted by this, as is not hidden. And it also includes if he inserted his finger into his anus without it becoming hidden; then wetness and odor are considered, and this is the correct view because it is not inserted from every angle, as in Sharh Qadikhan. And the exit is definitive. And it includes what exits contaminated due to the impurity it carries, so the statement 'exit of impurity' applies to it. Reflect, for with fatha it is more general, and Allah knows best. He said: (And it is an expression of the meaning) i.e., the cause is an expression of the meaning. Similarly, the meaning of his statement: 'it is not a condition in the operation of the cause nor a cause of the cause' is conjunct to his statement 'it is not a condition'. He said: (Because the correct view is that its substance is pure) said al-Ramli: I say it is problematic regarding whether it includes what exits from the anus in his words unless it is said: although its substance is not impure, it is contaminated, so it enters regardless of whether one reads 'najis' with fatha or kasra, since there is no difference between them linguistically. Reflect. He said: So their exit does not follow. And this.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically on the topic of wudu. The passage is divided into two main parts: first, a detailed discussion of the etiquette (adab) of wudu, listing fifteen points; second, a thorough examination of the nullifiers of wudu, focusing on the definition and ruling of the exit of impurity.

The author begins by noting that the commentary is from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli and clarifies that increasing washes beyond three is permissible if done for tranquility or with a new intention. He then lists fifteen points of adab, each with specific conditions and references to other works. For example, he emphasizes that refraining from speech is only praiseworthy when there is no genuine need, and that preparing wudu before the time is only for those without an excuse. He also notes that al-Zayla'i considered slapping the face with water as makruh, so avoiding it is sunnah, not merely adab.

Moving to the nullifier section, the author explains the key term 'najas' (impurity). He notes the technical distinction: with two fatha (najas) it means the substance of impurity; with kasra (najis) it means something that is not pure. In language, they are the same. The commentator prefers the kasra reading as more general.

The main ruling is that the nullifier of wudu is the exit (khuruj) of impurity, not the impurity itself. This is supported by the hadith: 'What is hadath? He said: What exits from the two passages.' The author discusses the causal reasoning: impurity is the cause (illa) of nullification, and exit is the cause of the cause (illat al-illa). This resolves a potential objection: if impurity itself were the nullifier, then no wudu would be valid because every skin has blood underneath. But since exit is a necessary condition, the ruling is sound.

The author then distinguishes two types of nullifiers: those exiting from the two passages (which are absolute nullifiers) and those from elsewhere. He discusses specific cases like a worm exiting from the rectum, penis, or vagina, noting that the worm is considered impure because it originates from impurity. However, wind exiting from the male organ or female vulva does not nullify wudu according to the correct view, as it is a spasm and not true wind; even if it were, it emanates from a place without impurity.

The commentary concludes with notes on the purity of the substance of wind and the ruling on inserting a finger into the anus without concealment, where wetness and odor are considered. The passage ends with a reminder that the term 'najis' with fatha is more comprehensive.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): Nullification; in wudu, taking it out from providing its intended benefit (permitting prayer).
الحدث (al-hadath): The state of impurity that nullifies wudu; defined in the hadith as 'what exits from the two passages'.
العلة (al-illa): The cause; in usul al-fiqh, the attribute that is the basis for a ruling. Here, impurity is the cause of nullification, and exit is the cause of the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following nullifies wudu?
A) A worm exiting from the anus
B) Wind exiting from the male organ
C) Inserting a finger into the anus without moisture
D) Blood emerging from a wound without flowing

Key Takeaway
The nullifier of wudu is the exit of impurity, not the impurity itself; wind from male/female organs does not nullify because it is pure and not from an impurity source.

The Nullifier Is the Impure Substance Upon Exit
Clarifying the effective cause of nullification of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on what nullifies wudu and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) that distinguishes between the impurity and its exit.

Core Idea
The nullifier of wudu is the external impurity, not its mere presence — exit is the key condition.

This lesson examines a fundamental question in Hanafi fiqh: what exactly causes wudu to break? The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a nuanced distinction between the impurity itself and its exit from the body. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of taharah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What exactly is being debated? The passage seeks to determine whether the nullifier of wudu is the impurity (najasah) itself or the act of its exit (khuruj).
The base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) uses the phrase 'خروج نجس' which implies exit of impurity.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Follow the logical argument: if impurity itself were the nullifier, no one could ever be in a state of purity since blood exists under every skin. Thus, exit must be a condition.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument used by the commentators of al-Hidayah.
3
Refine the view
The author from Fath al-Qadir suggests the nullifier is the external impurity because the property of removing purity only exists upon exit. The cause (illa) is impurity, exit is the cause of the cause.
This maintains that the impurity is the direct nullifier, but only when it exits.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'ما الحدث؟ قال: ما يخرج من السبيلين' is cited. The phrase 'ما يخرج' indicates that the thing which exits is the cause; exit is the path.
The hadith supports attributing the ruling to the exiting substance, not the exit alone.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position: the nullifier is the impure substance as it exits. Exit is a condition for the nullification to take effect.
This resolves the earlier objection: purity is possible because internal impurity does not nullify until it exits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنقض في الجسم فك تأليفه. وفي غيره إخراجه عن إفادة ما المقصود منه كإستباحة الصلاة في الوضوء، وأفاد بقوله «خروج نجس» أن الناقض خروجه لا عينه وعلل له في الكافي بأن الخروج علة الانتقاض وهي عبارة عن المعنى، وعلل شراح الهداية بأنها لو كانت نفسها ناقضة لما حصلت طهارة لشخص أصلاً لأن تحت كل جلدة دماً لكن قال في فتح القدير: الظاهر أن الناقض النجس الخارج وبينه بما حاصله أن الناقض هو المؤثر للنقض والضد هو المؤثر في رفع ضده وصفة النجاسة الرافعة للطهارة إنما هي بالخارج فالعلة للنقض هي النجاسة بشرط الخروج، وتأيد هذا بظاهر الحديث ما الحدث؟ قال: ما يخرج من السبيلين. فالعلة النجاسة والخروج علة العلة وإضافة الحكم إلى العلة أولى من إضافته إلى علة العلة فاندفع بهذا ما قالوا من لزوم عدم حصول طهارة لشخص على تقدير إضافة النقض إلى النجاسة إذ لا يلزم إلا لو قلنا بأن الخروج ليس شرطاً في عمل العلة ولا علة العلة.
Nullification in the body is the dissolution of its composition. In other things, it is taking it out of benefiting what is intended from it, like permitting prayer in wudu. And his statement 'exit of impurity' indicates that the nullifier is its exit, not its substance. It is reasoned in al-Kafi that exit is the cause of nullification, which is an expression of the meaning. The commentators of al-Hidayah reasoned that if it were itself a nullifier, then no one would ever attain purity because under every skin there is blood. But in Fath al-Qadir it is said: the apparent view is that the nullifier is the external impurity, and he explained it with the gist that the nullifier is what effects nullification, and the opposite is what effects the removal of its opposite, and the description of impurity that removes purity only exists externally. So the cause of nullification is impurity with the condition of exit. This is supported by the apparent meaning of the hadith: 'What is hadath?' He said: 'What exits from the two passages.' Therefore the cause is impurity, and exit is the cause of the cause. Attributing the ruling to the cause is more appropriate than attributing it to the cause of the cause. Thus what they said about the necessity of non-attainment of purity for anyone on the assumption of attributing nullification to impurity is refuted, because it would only follow if we said that exit is not a condition in the operation of the cause nor the cause of the cause.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a fundamental conceptual issue in Hanafi fiqh: what exactly nullifies wudu? The author states that nullification (naqd) in a physical body means dissolution, while in legal matters it means removal of benefit, like the permissibility of prayer through wudu. The crucial point is that the nullifier is the 'exit' (khuruj) of impurity, not the impurity itself. This is supported by reasoning: if the impurity itself were the nullifier, then no one could ever be pure because everyone has blood under their skin. However, the author cites Fath al-Qadir which holds that the nullifier is the external impurity itself, because the impurity's property of removing purity only exists upon exit. Thus, the 'illa (effective cause) is the impurity (najasah), and exit is the 'illa of the 'illa. The hadith 'what exits from the two passages' supports this, as it attributes the ruling to what exits. This resolves the objection that impurity would always nullify — because exit is a condition or a cause of the cause. The Hanafi position is that the nullifier is the impure substance that exits, and exit is a necessary condition.

Key Terms
ناقض (nāqiḍ): That which nullifies wudu; the effective cause of invalidation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view in this passage, what is the nullifier of wudu?
A) The impurity itself regardless of exit
B) The act of exiting alone
C) The impure substance as it exits
D) The intention to exit

Key Takeaway
The nullifier of wudu is the impure substance that exits; exit is a necessary condition for nullification.

Worms as Nullifiers of Wudu
Applying nullification principle to worms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a worm exiting from the two passages and identify the two opinions on what exactly nullifies.

Core Idea
A worm from the two passages nullifies wudu because it is impure, being generated from impurity.

The passage establishes a general principle: anything exiting from the front or back passages (sabīlayn) nullifies wudu. It then applies this to worms, but reveals a subtle intra-madhhab dispute: does the worm itself nullify, or only the moisture on it? This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The passage states that anything exiting from the two passages nullifies wudu absolutely. Recognise this as the baseline rule.
The two passages are the anus and the genitals.
2
Analyse the worm ruling
The worm is included in 'anything exiting' and thus nullifies. Note the consensus cited from al-Siraj, and the reasoning from al-Bada'i': the worm is impure because it originates from impurity (najasah).
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Two opinions exist: (1) the worm itself nullifies, (2) only the moisture on the worm nullifies. Al-Zayla'i chose the first view.
The second opinion is attributed to al-Asbijabi.
4
Apply the preferred view
In fatwa, the worm itself is the nullifier. The passage also analogises to a pebble (hasah) showing consistency.
The discussion clarifies that even if moisture is absent, the worm still nullifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل كلامه جميع النواقض الحقيقية وهو مجمل وهو قسمان: خارج من السبيلين وخارج من غيرهما. فالأول ناقض مطلقاً فتنقض الدودة الخارجة من الدبر والذكر والفرج كذا في الخانية. وفي السراج : إنه بالإجماع. فما في التبيين من أن الدودة الخارجة من فرجها على الخلاف ففيه نظر، وعلل في البدائع بكون الدودة ناقضة أنها نجسة لتولدها من النجاسة، وذكر الاسبيجابي أن فيها طريقتين : إحداهما ما ذكرناه والثانية أن الناقض ما عليها واختاره الزيلعي وهو في الحصاة مسلم. ولا يرد على المصنف الريح الخارجة من الذكر وفرج المرأة فإنها لا تنقض الوضوء على الصحيح لأن الخارج منهما اختلاج وليس بريح خارجة . ولو سلم فلیست بمنبعثة عن محل النجاسة والريح لا ينقض إلا لذلك لا لأن عينها نجسة لأن الصحيح أن عينها طاهرة حتى لو لبس سروال مبتلة أو ابتل من أليتيه الموضع الذي يمر به الريح فخرج الريح لا يتنجس وهو قول العامة وما نقل عن الحلواني من أنه كان لا يصلي راويله فورع منه كذا قالوا فاندفع بهذا ما ذكره مسكين في شرحه من أن كلام المصنف ليس على عمومه كما لا يخفى
And his speech covers all actual nullifiers, and it is general, and it is of two types: that which exits from the two passages and that which exits from other than them. The first is a nullifier absolutely, so the worm exiting from the anus, penis, and vagina nullifies; thus in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Siraj: it is by consensus. So what is in al-Tabyin that the worm exiting from her vagina is subject to disagreement is questionable. And it is reasoned in al-Bada'i' that the worm is a nullifier because it is impure, being generated from impurity. And al-Asbijabi mentioned that there are two ways: one is what we mentioned, and the second is that the nullifier is what is upon it (i.e., the moisture), and al-Zayla'i chose this, and it is accepted for the pebble. And there is no objection to the author from the wind exiting from the male organ and the female vagina, for it does not nullify wudu in the correct view, because what exits from them is a spasm and not exiting wind. Even if it were granted, it is not emitted from the place of impurity, and wind only nullifies for that reason, not because its substance is impure, for the correct view is that its substance is pure; so much so that if one wears a wet trouser or the place where the wind passes becomes wet from his buttocks, and the wind exits, it does not become impure. This is the saying of the general scholars. And what is reported from al-Halwani that he would not pray in his trousers out of scrupulousness is a matter of piety. So what Miskin mentioned in his commentary that the author's speech is not general is repelled by this, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses two specific types of nullifiers: worms and wind. The first type is anything exiting from the two passages (anus, penis, vagina), which nullifies wudu absolutely by consensus. A worm exiting from any of these is a nullifier because it is impure, being generated from impurity (najasah). There is a second opinion that the nullifier is the moisture on the worm, but the preferred view is that the worm itself is the nullifier. The second type is wind exiting from the male organ or female vagina. The correct Hanafi view is that this does not nullify wudu, because what exits is merely a spasm (ikhtilaj), not actual wind. Even if it were actual wind, it does not originate from the place of impurity (the anus), and wind only nullifies because it comes from the impure area, not because its substance is impure. In fact, the substance of wind is pure; if it passes over a wet garment, it does not make it impure. This rebuts the claim that the author's statement about wind not nullifying is not general.

Key Terms
سبيلين (sabīlayn): The two passages: the front (penis/vagina) and the back (anus).
دودة (dūdah): Worm; any small creature that may exit from the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, what is the nullifier when a worm exits from the two passages?
A) The moisture on the worm
B) The worm itself
C) Both the worm and the moisture
D) Neither, because worms are pure

Key Takeaway
A worm exiting from the two passages nullifies wudu by itself, not just its moisture; this is the mufta bihi Hanafi position.

Wind from Genitals Does Not Nullify Wudu
Clarifying wind from genitals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why wind exiting from the male organ or female vagina does not nullify wudu, and distinguish it from wind from the anus.

Core Idea
Wind from the genitals is a spasm, not real wind, and does not originate from the place of impurity.

The passage addresses a potential objection: what about wind exiting from the male organ or female vagina? The author clarifies that such wind does not nullify wudu, contrary to some misconceptions. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: it is either a spasm (ikhtilaj) or, even if real wind, it does not come from the impure area.

Guided Path
1
Identify the potential objection
The reader might think that the general rule 'anything from the two passages nullifies' includes wind from genitals. The author anticipates this and addresses it.
2
Analyse the two reasons
First reason: what exits from genitals is usually a spasm (ikhtilaj), not actual wind. Second: even if it were wind, it does not originate from the place of impurity (the anus).
Wind only nullifies because it comes from the impure area.
3
Examine the purity of wind
The passage clarifies that the substance of wind is pure. If wind passes over a wet garment, it does not make it impure. This supports the ruling.
This addresses a potential ruling about trouser wetness.
4
Rebut the objection to the author
Some commentators claimed the author's statement is not general. The passage refutes this by showing the consistency of the ruling.
The reference to Miskin's commentary is rebutted.

Conditions for Nullification by Inserted Objects
Ruling on inserted objects in the anus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of nullification when an object is inserted into the anus, and apply them to specific cases like finger, fly, and suppository.

Core Idea
Full insertion and moisture on exit nullify wudu; partial insertion requires moisture and odor.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the rulings on objects inserted into the anus. It establishes that the nullification of wudu depends on two key factors: whether the object is fully concealed (ghayb) and whether it exits with moisture (balla). We will examine the finger, fly, and suppository cases to understand these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key conditions
The passage shows that nullification depends on two factors: full insertion (ghayb) and moisture (balla) upon exit.
These are not cumulative; full insertion alone suffices.
2
Analyze the finger case
If the finger is not fully inserted, moisture and odor are considered; if fully inserted, nullification is absolute.
Partial insertion with moisture/odor also nullifies.
3
Extract the rule for full insertion
When an object is fully concealed (ghayb), its exit nullifies wudu regardless of moisture.
This is derived from the phrase 'if he conceals it, nullifies absolutely'.
4
Apply to fly and suppository
Both examples show that without moisture, nullification does not occur.
The suppository case adds a precautionary wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل أيضاً ما لو أدخل أصبعه في دبره ولم يغيبها فإنه تعتبر فيه البلة والرائحة وهو الصحيح لأنه ليس بداخل من كل وجه كذا في شرح قاضيخان . واستفيد منه أنه إذا غيبه نقض مطلقاً، وكذا الذباب إذا طار ودخل في الدبر وخرج من غير بلة لا ينقض، وكذا المحقنة إذا أدخلها ثم أخرجها إن لم يكن عليها بلة لا تنقض والأحوط أن يتوضأ كذا في منية المصلي. وفي الخانية : وإذا أقطر في إحليله دهناً ثم عاد فلا وضوء عليه بخلاف ما إذا احتقن بدهن ثم عاد ا هـ . والفرق بينهما إن في الثاني اختلط الدهن بالنجاسة بخلاف الاحليل للحائل عند أبي حنيفة كذا في فتح القدير. فعلى هذا فعدم النقض قوله فقط وقد صرح به في المحيط فقال : لا ينقض عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لأبي يوسف. والإحليل بكسر الهمزة مجرى البول من الذكر وفي الولوالجية: وكل شيء إذا غيبه ثم أخرجه أو خرج فعليه الوضوء وقضاء الصوم لأنه كان داخلاً مطلقاً فترتب عليه الخروج، وكل شيء إذا أدخل بعضه وطرفه خارج لا ينقض الوضوء وليس عليه قضاء الصوم لأنه غير داخل مطلقاً فلا يترتب عليه الخروج .ا.هـ. والكلية الثانية مقيدة بعدم البلة كما في المحيط . وفي البدائع : لو احتشت في الفرج الداخل ونفذت البلة إلى الجانب الآخر فإن كانت القطنة عالية أم محاذية لحرف الفرج كان حدثاً لوجود الخروج، وإن كانت القطنة متسفلة عنه لا ينقض لعدم الخروج. وفي منية المصلي وإن كانت احتشت في الفرج الخارج فابتل داخل الحشو انتقض نفذ أو لم ينفذ. وفي التبيين : وإن حشي إحليله بقطنة فخروجه بابتلال خارجه.
And it also includes the case if one inserts his finger into his anus and does not conceal it, then moisture and odor are considered, and this is the correct view because it is not inserted from all aspects, as in Sharh Qadikhan. And it is derived from this that if he conceals it, it nullifies absolutely. Likewise, if a fly flies and enters the anus and exits without moisture, it does not nullify. Likewise, a suppository if inserted and then removed, if there is no moisture on it, it does not nullify, but it is precautionary to perform wudu, as in Munyat al-Musalli. In al-Khaniyya: if he drips oil into his urethra and it returns, there is no wudu upon him, unlike if he gives an enema with oil and it returns. The difference between them is that in the second case the oil mixes with impurity, unlike the urethra due to the barrier according to Abu Hanifa, as in Fath al-Qadir. So on this, the non-nullification is his statement only, and it is explicitly stated in al-Muhit: 'It does not nullify according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to Abu Yusuf.' Al-Ihlil (with kasra on alif) is the passage of urine from the penis. In al-Walwalijiyya: everything that is concealed then taken out or exits requires wudu and qada of fast because it was absolutely inside, so exit follows; everything that is partially inserted with its tip outside does not nullify wudu and does not require qada of fast because it is not absolutely inside, so no exit follows. The second general rule is conditioned by absence of moisture, as in al-Muhit. In al-Bada'i': if she packs the inner vagina and moisture penetrates to the other side, then if the cotton is high or level with the labia, it is hadath due to existence of exit; if the cotton is below that, it does not nullify due to absence of exit. In Munyat al-Musalli: if she packs the outer vagina and the inside of the packing becomes wet, it nullifies whether it penetrates or not. In al-Tabyin: if he packs his urethra with cotton, then its exit is by wetting its outside.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on objects inserted into the body. The key principle is that if an object is completely concealed (inserted fully) and then removed, wudu is nullified if there is moisture (impurity) on it; otherwise not. Partial insertion does not nullify. Specific cases: inserting a finger into the anus without full insertion — moisture and odor matter. A fly entering and exiting without moisture does not nullify. A suppository nullifies only if it returns with moisture. Oil dripped into the urethra does not nullify upon return because of a barrier (the urethra does not contain impurity), unlike an enema where oil mixes with impurity. Abu Hanifa holds it does not nullify; Abu Yusuf disagrees. The general rule: anything completely inside then coming out nullifies wudu and requires qada of fast; partial insertion does not. For a woman packing the vagina, the ruling depends on whether the packing is inside the inner vagina and moisture reaches the other side, or if it is in the outer area.

Key Terms
غيب (ghayb): Full concealment; inserting an object completely so that no part remains outside.
بلة (balla): Moisture, wetness; often indicates impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a suppository is inserted and removed without any moisture?
A) Wudu is nullified because something entered and exited.
B) Wudu is not nullified, but it is precautionary to perform wudu.
C) Wudu is nullified only if the suppository was fully inserted.
D) Wudu is not nullified, and no precaution is needed.

Key Takeaway
Nullification of wudu by an inserted object requires either full concealment (ghayb) or the presence of moisture or odor upon exit.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Oil in the Urethra
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on urethra oil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between oil dripping into the urethra and an oil enema, and state Abu Hanifa's position on nullification.

Core Idea
Oil returning from the urethra does not nullify wudu due to a barrier; enema oil mixes with impurity and nullifies.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding oil that is introduced into the urethra (ihlil) and then returns. The key distinction is that the urethra has a barrier (ha'il) that separates it from impurity, whereas the rectum does not. We will analyze the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Oil is dripped into the urethra and returns; also an oil enema is given and returns.
Both involve oil entering and exiting.
2
Identify the difference
In the urethra, there is a barrier that prevents the oil from mixing with impurity; in the enema, the oil mixes with impurity.
This is the basis of the ikhtilaf.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa rules no nullification for urethra oil; Abu Yusuf rules it nullifies.
Both agree on the enema case (nullification).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's, as stated in al-Muhit and Fath al-Qadir.
The barrier (ha'il) is a valid legal reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rulings for Female Vaginal Packing and Urethra Packing
Rulings for vaginal and urethra packing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inner and outer vaginal packing, and state when wudu is nullified based on moisture and location.

Core Idea
Nullification from vaginal packing depends on whether the packing is in the inner or outer vagina and whether moisture penetrates.

This passage extends the principles of nullification to more complex cases: female vaginal packing and male urethra packing. The rulings consider the location of the packing (inner vs outer vagina) and whether moisture penetrates through. We will examine these cases to understand how the conditions of ghayb and balla apply.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule from al-Walwalijiyya
Anything fully concealed (ghayb) and then removed requires wudu and qada of fast; partial insertion does not.
The second part is conditioned on absence of moisture.
2
Analyze inner vaginal packing
If cotton is packed in the inner vagina and moisture penetrates to the other side, and the cotton is high or level with the labia, it is hadath (nullifies) because moisture exits.
If cotton is below the labia, no nullification because no exit.
3
Analyze outer vaginal packing
If packed in the outer vagina and the inside of the packing becomes wet, it nullifies whether moisture penetrates or not.
The outer vagina is considered external, so wetting of the packing itself constitutes exit.
4
Apply to male urethra packing
If cotton is packed into the urethra, its exit is determined by wetting of its outside.
This aligns with the barrier concept: only when moisture appears externally does it nullify.

Nullification of Wudu for the Hermaphrodite (Khuntha)
Ruling on khuntha nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on nullification of wudu for a khuntha and apply it regardless of whether the person's sex is known.

Core Idea
A khuntha's wudu is nullified by urine from either passage, even if the true sex is unknown.

This lesson addresses a special case of ritual purity: a hermaphrodite (khuntha) possesses both male and female genitalia. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that any urine exit, whether from the male organ or the female opening, nullifies wudu. This applies even if the person's biological sex is discovered later or remains ambiguous. The precautionary approach ensures that no impurity is overlooked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Recognize that the khuntha has both male and female passages. The ruling depends on whether the true sex is known or not.
2
Understand the two scenarios
If the khuntha is later identified as a woman, the male organ is treated like a wound; if a man, the female organ is like a wound. In either case, urine from the 'other' passage nullifies wudu as if from a wound.
The term 'wound' here means that the exit is not a natural passage, so its discharge is a nullifier.
3
State the general rule
Regardless of identification, urine exiting from either passage nullifies wudu. This is the relied-upon position stated in al-Tabyin and preferred by most scholars.
The rule applies whether the urine flows or not, and whether the person's condition is clarified or not.
4
Apply the precautionary principle
For the ambiguous khuntha (mushkil), the precaution is to consider any exit as nullifying, as stated in al-Tawshih.
This avoids any possibility of praying without valid wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير : والخنثى إذا تبين أنه امرأة فذكره كالجرح أو رجل ففرجه كالجرح وينقض في الآخر بالظهور لكن قال في التبيين وأكثرهم على إيجاب الوضوء عليه. فحاصله أن الخنثى ينتقض وضوءه بخروج البول من فرجيه جميعاً سال أو لا، تبين حاله أو لا. وفي التوشيح: يؤخذ في الخنثى المشكل بالأحوط وهو النقض، وأما المفضاة وهي التي صار مسلك البول والغائط منها واحداً أو التي صار مسلك بولها ووطئها واحداً، فيستحب لها الوضوء من الريح ولا يجب لأن اليقين لا يزول بالشك. وعن محمد وجوبه وبه أخذ أبو حفص للاحتياط ورجحه في فتح القدير بأن الغالب في الريح كونها من الدبر بل لا نسبة لكونها من القبل به فيفيد غلبة ظن تقرب من اليقين وهو خصوصاً في موضع الاحتياط له ناظر إلى الوضوء فقط بخلاف ما قبله
And in Fath al-Qadir: If a hermaphrodite (khuntha) is identified as a woman, then her male organ is like a wound; if a man, then his vagina is like a wound, and it nullifies in the other by appearance. But in al-Tabyin and most scholars obligate wudu upon him. The summary is that the wudu of a khuntha is nullified by urine exiting from both his/her passages, whether it flows or not, whether his/her condition is clarified or not. In al-Tawshih: for the ambiguous (mushkil) khuntha, one takes the precautionary measure, which is nullification. As for the mafdaah (one whose urinary and fecal passages have become one, or whose urinary and coital passages have become one), it is recommended for her to perform wudu from wind and not obligatory, because certainty is not removed by doubt. According to Muhammad, it is obligatory, and Abu Hafs adopted it for precaution, and he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir because the majority of wind comes from the anus; indeed, there is no proportion of it coming from the front, so it yields a strong probability close to certainty, and especially in a place of precaution, it looks only to wudu, unlike what came before.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage deals with two special cases: the hermaphrodite (khuntha) and the woman with a prolapsed uterus (mafdaah). For the khuntha, the correct view is that wudu is nullified by urine exiting from either the male or female passage, regardless of whether the person's true sex is known. The precautionary view is to treat any exit as nullifying. For the mafdaah (where the urinary and fecal passages have merged, or urinary and coital passages have merged), the ruling on wind is that it is recommended (mustahabb) to perform wudu but not obligatory, because certainty (of remaining in wudu) is not overridden by doubt. However, Muhammad al-Shaybani held it obligatory, and Abu Hafs adopted this for precaution. The author of Fath al-Qadir preferred the view of obligation because it is more likely that wind from such a woman originates from the anus (the usual source of impurity) rather than the front. This probability is close to certainty, and in matters of precaution, one should act upon it. However, this is specific to wudu, not to other matters like fasting.

Key Terms
خنثى (khunthā): A hermaphrodite with both male and female genitalia.
مشكل (mushkil): Ambiguous; in this context, a khuntha whose true sex is unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a khuntha whose true sex is unknown (mushkil)?
A) Wudu is nullified only if urine exits from the male organ
B) Wudu is nullified only if urine exits from the female organ
C) Wudu is nullified by urine from either passage, as a precaution
D) Wudu is not nullified because of uncertainty

Key Takeaway
For a khuntha, any urine exit, regardless of sex identification, nullifies wudu; precaution is advised for ambiguous cases.

Ruling on Wind Nullification for the Mafdaah Woman
Ruling on mafdaah wind nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on wudu nullification for a mafdaah woman and evaluate the ikhtilaf between istihbab and wujub.

Core Idea
For a mafdaah, wudu from wind is recommended but the preferred view makes it obligatory due to strong probability.

A mafdaah woman suffers from a condition where her urinary and fecal passages have merged, or her urinary and coital passages have merged. When she passes wind, there is doubt whether it originated from the anus (the regular source) or the front passage. The Hanafi school generally holds that wudu is recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory because certainty is not removed by doubt. However, Imam Muhammad considered it obligatory, and later scholars like Abu Hafs and Ibn al-Humam preferred this view for precaution, citing the strong probability that wind comes from the anus.

Guided Path
1
Define mafdaah
A woman with merged passages: either urinary and fecal passages become one, or urinary and coital passages become one. This causes uncertainty about the source of wind.
The term 'mafdaah' literally means 'having a merged path'.
2
State the default Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it is recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory to perform wudu upon passing wind, because the principle 'certainty is not removed by doubt' means that the original state of purity remains until proven otherwise.
The doubt is about whether the wind is from the anus (impure) or the front (not impure for wudu purposes).
3
Present the opposing view
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani held that it is obligatory (wajib) to perform wudu, and Abu Hafs adopted this for precaution. This view is based on the high probability that wind from such a woman originates from the anus.
Probability here is considered close to certainty.
4
Analyze the tarjih in Fath al-Qadir
Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir preferred Muhammad's view because the majority of wind originates from the anus, and there is no proportion from the front. This strong probability justifies obligation, especially in matters of precaution.
The precaution is specifically for wudu, not for fasting or other acts.
5
Understand the limitation
This ruling of obligation is limited to wudu. It does not extend to other matters like nullification of fasting or impurity of clothes.
The passage explicitly says 'it looks only to wudu, unlike what came before.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير : والخنثى إذا تبين أنه امرأة فذكره كالجرح أو رجل ففرجه كالجرح وينقض في الآخر بالظهور لكن قال في التبيين وأكثرهم على إيجاب الوضوء عليه. فحاصله أن الخنثى ينتقض وضوءه بخروج البول من فرجيه جميعاً سال أو لا، تبين حاله أو لا. وفي التوشيح: يؤخذ في الخنثى المشكل بالأحوط وهو النقض، وأما المفضاة وهي التي صار مسلك البول والغائط منها واحداً أو التي صار مسلك بولها ووطئها واحداً، فيستحب لها الوضوء من الريح ولا يجب لأن اليقين لا يزول بالشك. وعن محمد وجوبه وبه أخذ أبو حفص للاحتياط ورجحه في فتح القدير بأن الغالب في الريح كونها من الدبر بل لا نسبة لكونها من القبل به فيفيد غلبة ظن تقرب من اليقين وهو خصوصاً في موضع الاحتياط له ناظر إلى الوضوء فقط بخلاف ما قبله
And in Fath al-Qadir: If a hermaphrodite (khuntha) is identified as a woman, then her male organ is like a wound; if a man, then his vagina is like a wound, and it nullifies in the other by appearance. But in al-Tabyin and most scholars obligate wudu upon him. The summary is that the wudu of a khuntha is nullified by urine exiting from both his/her passages, whether it flows or not, whether his/her condition is clarified or not. In al-Tawshih: for the ambiguous (mushkil) khuntha, one takes the precautionary measure, which is nullification. As for the mafdaah (one whose urinary and fecal passages have become one, or whose urinary and coital passages have become one), it is recommended for her to perform wudu from wind and not obligatory, because certainty is not removed by doubt. According to Muhammad, it is obligatory, and Abu Hafs adopted it for precaution, and he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir because the majority of wind comes from the anus; indeed, there is no proportion of it coming from the front, so it yields a strong probability close to certainty, and especially in a place of precaution, it looks only to wudu, unlike what came before.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage deals with two special cases: the hermaphrodite (khuntha) and the woman with a prolapsed uterus (mafdaah). For the khuntha, the correct view is that wudu is nullified by urine exiting from either the male or female passage, regardless of whether the person's true sex is known. The precautionary view is to treat any exit as nullifying. For the mafdaah (where the urinary and fecal passages have merged, or urinary and coital passages have merged), the ruling on wind is that it is recommended (mustahabb) to perform wudu but not obligatory, because certainty (of remaining in wudu) is not overridden by doubt. However, Muhammad al-Shaybani held it obligatory, and Abu Hafs adopted this for precaution. The author of Fath al-Qadir preferred the view of obligation because it is more likely that wind from such a woman originates from the anus (the usual source of impurity) rather than the front. This probability is close to certainty, and in matters of precaution, one should act upon it. However, this is specific to wudu, not to other matters like fasting.

Key Terms
مفضاة (mafdāh): A woman whose urinary/fecal passages have merged into one, or urinary/coital passages merged.
استحباب (istihbāb): Recommendation; an act that is rewarded but not required.
وجوب (wujūb): Obligation; an act that is required, omission is sinful.

Nullification of Wudu by Exit of Objects: The Condition of Wetness
Ruling with explanation and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that exit of an object from the private parts nullifies wudu only if accompanied by wetness or impurity, and distinguish the urethra case due to the barrier (hail).

Core Idea
Exit without wetness does not nullify wudu — the barrier in the urethra changes the ruling.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores a nuanced detail of wudu nullification: not every exit of an object from the private parts breaks wudu. The key is whether the object exits with wetness (bulla) or impurity. A special case is oil inserted into the urethra, where a barrier prevents mixing, leading to a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply these rules confidently and understand the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
Note that the passage begins with a general rule: fully concealed objects that exit nullify wudu absolutely. However, this is immediately qualified by examples.
The initial statement 'if he concealed it, it nullifies absolutely' is tempered by later conditions.
2
Apply the condition of wetness
Observe the examples of a fly and an enema: if they exit without wetness, they do not nullify wudu. This establishes the key condition.
The term 'bulla' (wetness) is critical; nullification only occurs if wetness or impurity is present.
3
Analyze the special case of the urethra
Read the discussion on oil dropped into the urethra. Unlike an enema, the oil does not mix with impurity due to a barrier (hail).
This barrier is the distinguishing factor that changes the ruling.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify that Abu Hanifa holds no nullification for the urethra case, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Note the mufta bihi position.
The text clearly favors Abu Hanifa's view: 'فعلى هذا فعدم النقض قوله فقط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستفيد منه أنه إذا غيبه نقض مطلقاً، وكذا الذباب إذا طار ودخل في الدبر وخرج من غير بلة لا ينقض، وكذا المحقنة إذا أدخلها ثم أخرجها إن لم يكن عليها بلة لا تنقض والأحوط أن يتوضأ كذا في منية المصلي. وفي الخانية : وإذا أقطر في إحليله دهناً ثم عاد فلا وضوء عليه بخلاف ما إذا احتقن بدهن ثم عاد ا هـ . والفرق بينهما إن في الثاني اختلط الدهن بالنجاسة بخلاف الاحليل للحائل عند أبي حنيفة كذا في فتح القدير. فعلى هذا فعدم النقض قوله فقط وقد صرح به في المحيط فقال : لا ينقض عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لأبي يوسف. والإحليل بكسر الهمزة مجرى البول من الذكر وفي الولوالجية: وكل شيء إذا غيبه ثم أخرجه أو خرج فعليه الوضوء وقضاء الصوم لأنه كان داخلاً مطلقاً فترتب عليه الخروج، وكل شيء إذا أدخل بعضه وطرفه خارج لا ينقض الوضوء وليس عليه قضاء الصوم لأنه غير داخل مطلقاً فلا يترتب عليه الخروج .ا.هـ. والكلية الثانية مقيدة بعدم البلة كما في المحيط.
And it is derived from it that if he concealed it, it nullifies absolutely. Similarly, a fly if it flies and enters the anus and exits without wetness does not nullify. Similarly, an enema if he inserts it then removes it, if there is no wetness on it, it does not nullify, but it is precautionary to perform wudu, as in Maniyat al-Musalli. In al-Khaniyya: If he drops oil into his urethra then it returns, there is no wudu upon him, unlike if he administers an enema with oil then it returns. The difference between them is that in the second the oil mixes with impurity, unlike the urethra due to the barrier according to Abu Hanifa, as in Fath al-Qadir. Upon this, the non-nullification is only his statement, and he stated it in al-Muhit: 'It does not nullify according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to Abu Yusuf.' Al-Ihlil (with kasr of hamza) is the passage of urine from the male. In al-Walwalijiyya: And every thing that is concealed then he removes it or it comes out, wudu is required and qada of the fast, because it was inside absolutely, so exit follows it. And every thing that is partially inserted while its tip is outside, it does not nullify wudu and qada of the fast is not required, because it is not inside absolutely, so exit does not follow it. The second general rule is qualified by the absence of wetness, as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation. It begins by establishing that if something is fully concealed inside the body (e.g., in the anus) and then removed, it nullifies wudu unconditionally. However, it then qualifies that if the object exits without any wetness (bulla), it does not break wudu. Examples given include a fly entering the anus and exiting dry, or an enema inserted and removed without wetness. In such cases, it is still recommended (ahwat) to renew wudu. The text then moves to a specific issue from al-Khaniyya: if oil is inserted into the urethra (ihlil) and then expelled, no wudu is required according to Abu Hanifa, unlike an enema with oil where wudu is required because the oil mixes with impurity. The difference is the presence of a barrier (hail) in the urethra that prevents mixing. This leads to a intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds no nullification, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's. The text then defines ihlil as the urinary passage. Finally, two general rules from al-Walwalijiyya are cited: (1) anything fully inserted and then exiting requires wudu and qada of fast; (2) anything partially inserted with the tip outside does not nullify wudu or require qada. The second rule is qualified by the condition of no wetness. These rules serve as comprehensive principles for nullification by insertion and exit.

Key Terms
بلة (bulla): Wetness or moisture that may be present on an object after removal from the body.
إحليل (ihlil): The urinary passage of the male, specifically the urethra.
حائل (hail): A barrier that prevents the mixing of inserted oil with impurity inside the urethra.
محقنة (mihqana): An enema, a device used to inject fluid into the rectum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wudu if an enema is inserted and then removed without any wetness?
A) Wudu is nullified
B) Wudu is not nullified, but it is precautionary to renew wudu
C) Wudu is nullified only if the enema was with oil
D) Wudu remains valid and no renewal is recommended

Key Takeaway
Exit of an object from the private parts nullifies wudu only if accompanied by wetness or impurity; the urethra has a unique ruling due to the barrier (hail).

Special Cases of Nullification: Packing Cotton, Eunuch, Hermaphrodite, and Mufda
A compilation of rulings on unusual nullification cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for four special cases that affect wudu nullification: packing cotton, eunuch, hermaphrodite, and mufda woman.

Core Idea
In special cases, wudu nullification depends on control, location, and probability.

This lesson presents rulings from multiple Hanafi sources on unusual situations involving packing cotton, eunuchs, hermaphrodites, and women with fused passages. These rulings apply general principles like certainty and presumption to unique circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four special cases
List the cases: packing cotton (qutna), eunuch (majbub), hermaphrodite (khuntha), and mufda woman. Each has a distinct rule.
The passage groups them but we treat separately.
2
Analyze the packing cotton rulings
For internal packing: nullification occurs if the cotton is high or parallel to the edge and wetness penetrates; if lower, no nullification. For external packing: nullification if the inner cotton becomes wet.
Location and wetness are key.
3
Understand the eunuch ruling
If he can control the discharge, it is urine and nullifies wudu. If he cannot, it does not nullify unless it flows.
Control distinguishes voluntary from involuntary.
4
Apply the hermaphrodite ruling
Wudu is nullified by exit of urine from either organ, whether flow is controlled or not, and regardless of gender clarification. Precaution dictates nullification.
Certainty of impurity prevails.
5
Examine the mufda woman ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis hold wudu from wind is mustahabb, citing doubt; Imam Muhammad and the mufta bihi view hold it is wajib due to strong presumption that wind comes from the anus.
The dispute is resolved by preferring the stronger presumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البدائع : لو احتشت في الفرج الداخل ونفذت البلة إلى الجانب الآخر فإن كانت القطنة عالية أم محاذية لحرف الفرج كان حدثاً لوجود الخروج، وإن كانت القطنة متسفلة عنه لا ينقض لعدم الخروج. وفي منية المصلي وإن كانت احتشت في الفرج الخارج فابتل داخل الحشو انتقض نفذ أو لم ينفذ. وفي التبيين : وإن حشي إحليله بقطنة فخروجه بابتلال خارجه. وفي الخانية : المجبوب إذا خرج منه ما يشبه البول إن كان قادراً على إمساكه إن شاء أمسكه وإن شاء أرسله فهو بول ينقض الوضوء، وإن كان لا يقدر على إمساكه لا ينقض ما لم يسل. وفي فتح القدير : والخنثى إذا تبين أنه امرأة فذكره كالجرح أو رجل ففرجه كالجرح وينقض في الآخر بالظهور لكن قال في التبيين وأكثرهم على إيجاب الوضوء عليه. فحاصله أن الخنثى ينتقض وضوءه بخروج البول من فرجيه جميعاً سال أو لا، تبين حاله أو لا. وفي التوشيح: يؤخذ في الخنثى المشكل بالأحوط وهو النقض، وأما المفضاة وهي التي صار مسلك البول والغائط منها واحداً أو التي صار مسلك بولها ووطئها واحداً، فيستحب لها الوضوء من الريح ولا يجب لأن اليقين لا يزول بالشك. وعن محمد وجوبه وبه أخذ أبو حفص للاحتياط ورجحه في فتح القدير بأن الغالب في الريح كونها من الدبر بل لا نسبة لكونها من القبل به فيفيد غلبة ظن تقرب من اليقين وهو خصوصاً في موضع الاحتياط له ناظر إلى الوضوء فقط بخلاف ما قبله قوله : (والكلية الثانية مقيدة بعدم البلة) قال الرملي : أقول هذا إنما يتأتى في نقض الوضوء فأما في الصوم فلا لتعلقه بالدخول فقط، ثم في الكلية الأولى إشكال أنه يلزم على إطلاقها أن نحكم بنقيض الوضوء بغير خارج نجس إذا خرج ذلك الشيء غير مبتل فتأمل. قوله : (لكن) قال في التبيين (الخ قال في النهر إلا أن الذي ينبغي التعويل عليه هو حكم اليقين فترجح الوجوب ا هـ . لكن ينبغي ترجيحه فيها بالمعنى الأول، أما بالمعنى الثاني فلا، لأن الصحيح عدم النقض بالريح الخارجة من الفرج. وقوله في الهداية الاحتمال خروجه من الدبر» يشير إلى المعنى الأول ولها حكمان آخران الأول لو طلقت ثلاثاً وتزوجت بآخر لا تحل للأول ما لم تحبل لاحتمال الوطء في الدبر. الثاني يحرم على زوجها جماعها إلا أن يمكنه إتيانها في قبلها من غير تعد كذا في فتح القدير. وينبغي أن يختصا بها بالمعنى الأول، وأما بالمعنى الثاني فلا كما يفيده التعليل المذكور.
In al-Bada'i': If she packs cotton inside the internal vagina and the wetness penetrates to the other side, if the cotton is high or parallel to the edge of the vagina, it is an event (hadath) due to exit; if the cotton is lower than it, it does not nullify due to no exit. In Maniyat al-Musalli: And if she packs cotton in the external vagina and the inside of the packing becomes wet, it nullifies whether it penetrates or not. In al-Tabyin: And if he packs his urethra with cotton, its exit is by wetting its outside. In al-Khaniyya: The eunuch (majbub) if what resembles urine comes out of him, if he is able to withhold it, he can withhold it if he wishes or release it, it is urine that nullifies wudu; if he is unable to withhold it, it does not nullify unless it flows. In Fath al-Qadir: The hermaphrodite (khuntha) if it becomes clear that he is a woman, then his male organ is like a wound; or if a man, then his female organ is like a wound, and it nullifies in the other by appearance, but al-Tabyin and most scholars require wudu upon him. The conclusion is that the hermaphrodite's wudu is nullified by exit of urine from both organs, whether it flows or not, whether his condition is clarified or not. In al-Tawshih: In the ambiguous hermaphrodite, precaution is taken, which is nullification. As for the mufda, who is one whose path of urine and feces has become one, or whose path of urine and intercourse has become one, wudu from wind is recommended (mustahabb) for her, not obligatory, because certainty is not removed by doubt. From Muhammad, it is obligatory, and Abu Hafs adopted it for precaution. It was preferred in Fath al-Qadir because the majority of wind comes from the anus; indeed, there is no proportion for it to be from the front, so it yields a strong presumption approaching certainty, especially in a place of precaution. This is specific to wudu only, unlike what preceded. Comment of Ramli: I say this applies to nullification of wudu, but for fasting it does not, because it relates only to entry. Then in the first general rule there is a problem: its absolute application would require us to rule nullification of wudu by a non-najis exit if that thing exits without wetness, so reflect. Then the text says: 'but' al-Tabyin said... al-Nahr said that what should be relied upon is the ruling of certainty, so obligation is preponderant. But it should be preponderant in it according to the first meaning; as for the second meaning, no, because the correct view is non-nullification by wind exiting the front. And his statement in al-Hidayah 'the possibility of its exit from the anus' indicates the first meaning. And she has two other rulings: first, if she is divorced thrice and marries another, she does not become lawful for the first unless she becomes pregnant due to the possibility of intercourse in the anus; second, it is prohibited for her husband to have intercourse with her unless he can come to her front without transgression, as in Fath al-Qadir. And these two rulings should be specific to her according to the first meaning, but not according to the second, as indicated by the mentioned reasoning.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on various special cases affecting nullification of wudu. The first case concerns packing cotton in the vagina: if the cotton is placed in the internal part and wetness penetrates to the other side, it constitutes a hadath (event) and breaks wudu if the cotton is at or above the edge of the vagina, but not if it is below. If packing is in the external vagina and the internal cotton becomes wet, it breaks wudu whether the wetness penetrates or not. For the urethra, packing cotton causes nullification only if the outside of the cotton becomes wet. Next, for a eunuch (majbub), if a urine-like fluid emerges and he can control its release, it is considered urine and breaks wudu; if he cannot control it, it does not break wudu unless it flows. For a hermaphrodite (khuntha), once the gender is determined, the opposite organ is treated like a wound; nullification occurs by appearance on the other side. Most scholars require wudu. The summary is that a hermaphrodite's wudu is nullified by urine exit from both organs regardless of flow or clarity of gender. The precaution is to assume nullification. For the mufda woman (fused passages), wudu from passing wind is recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory because certainty is not removed by doubt. However, Imam Muhammad held it is obligatory, and Abu Hafs adopted this for precaution. The preferred view in Fath al-Qadir is that since wind predominantly comes from the anus, there is a strong presumption that it is from there, triggering wudu. This ruling is specific to wudu only, not fasting. The text then discusses a comment by Ramli clarifying that the first general rule (full insertion = nullification) would lead to a problem if applied absolutely, because if a non-najis object exits without wetness, it would also nullify, which is not the case. The passage ends with two additional rulings related to the mufda: if she is divorced thrice and remarries, she does not become lawful to the first husband unless she becomes pregnant (due to possibility of anal intercourse), and her husband is prohibited from sex unless he can access her front without transgression.

Key Terms
المجبوب (al-majbub): A eunuch, a man whose testicles have been removed or whose male organ is cut off, resulting in possible urinary incontinence.
الخنثى (al-khuntha): A hermaphrodite, a person with both male and female reproductive organs.
المفضاة (al-mufda): A woman whose urinary and anal passages have become fused, or whose urinary and vaginal passages have become fused.
القطنة (al-qutna): Cotton used for packing the private parts to absorb discharge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wudu for a eunuch (majbub) who has urine-like discharge that he cannot control?
A) It nullifies wudu immediately
B) It does not nullify unless it flows
C) It nullifies only if he can control it
D) It never nullifies wudu

Key Takeaway
In special cases, Hanafi fiqh applies nuanced principles—control, location, wetness, and probability—to determine wudu nullification when standard rules face ambiguity.

Three Cases of Wudu Nullification: Split Penis, Hemorrhoid, and Urine Descent
Three rulings on nullification of wudu with an intra-Hanafi dispute on the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for wudu nullification in three specific scenarios: split penis, hemorrhoid reinsertion, and urine descent to the foreskin, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the last.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, wudu nullification depends on the location and manner of impurity exit.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents three nuanced rulings on wudu nullification. You will learn the conditions for nullification in a split penis, a protruding hemorrhoid, and when urine reaches the foreskin. The last case involves a significant intra-Hanafi debate, making it a valuable exercise in tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
Read the passage and list the three distinct scenarios that are being ruled upon: split penis, hemorrhoid reinsertion, and urine descent to the foreskin.
The passage also touches on a worm exiting the anus as a sub-case.
2
Analyze the split penis ruling
Note the distinction between the two heads: one connected to the urethra (nullification by appearance) and the other (nullification only after flow beyond the head).
The key is whether the urine exits from the usual path or a secondary one.
3
Understand the hemorrhoid ruling
If the hemorrhoid is manually reinserted, wudu is nullified due to impurity sticking to the hand; if it re-enters by itself (e.g., sneezing), no nullification.
The act of wiping impurity with the hand is the trigger.
4
Trace the foreskin dispute
For urine reaching the foreskin, there is a difference of opinion. The text states the correct view (sahih) is nullification, but al-Zayla'i objects based on ghusl analogy.
Follow the objection and response carefully.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Assess the strength of the objection and the response, noting that later authorities (Fath al-Qadir) challenge the response, leaving the issue unresolved.
The mufta bihi is still nullification, but the debate is acknowledged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان بذكره شق له رأسان إحداهما يخرج منه ماء يسيل في مجرى الذكر والأخرى في غيره، ففي الأول ينقض بالظهور وفي الثاني بالسيلان. وفي التوشيح باسوري خرج من دبره فإن عالجه بيده أو بخرقة حتى أدخله تنتقض طهارته لأنه يلتزق بيده شيء من النجاسة إلا إن عطس فدخل بنفسه. وذكر الحلواني : إن تيقن خروج الدبر تنتقض طهارته بخروج النجاسة من الباطن إلى الظاهر ويخرج على هذا لو خرج بعض الدودة فدخلت ا هـ . ثم الخروج في السبيلين يتحقق بالظهور فلو نزل البول إلى قصبة الذكر لا ينقض وإلى القلفة فيه خلاف والصحيح النقض، واستشكله الزيلعي هنا بأنهم قالوا لا يجب على الجنب إيصال الماء إليه لأنه خلقه كقصبة الذكر. وأجاب عنه في الغسل بأن الصحيح وجوب الإيصال على الجنب فلا إشكال، لكن في فتح القدير الصحيح المعتمد عدم وجوب الايصال في الغسل للحرج لا لأنه خلقة فلا يرد الإشكال
And if his penis has a split with two heads, one of which water (urine) flows through the urethra, and the other through something else, in the first it nullifies by appearance, and in the second by flowing. In al-Tawshih: A hemorrhoid came out of his anus; if he treats it with his hand or a cloth until he inserts it, his purification is nullified because something of impurity sticks to his hand, unless he sneezes and it enters by itself. And al-Halwani mentioned: If he is certain of the exit of the anus, his purification is nullified by the exit of impurity from the inside to the outside, and based on this, if part of a worm comes out and re-enters, [the same applies]. Then, exit from the two passages is realized by appearance: if urine descends to the shaft of the penis, it does not nullify; to the foreskin, there is a difference of opinion, and the correct view is nullification. Al-Zayla'i objected here saying they said: Water does not have to reach it for a junub because it is created like the shaft of the penis. And he answered regarding ghusl that the correct view is that it is obligatory to deliver water for a junub, so there is no problem. But in Fath al-Qadir, the correct relied-upon view is that there is no obligation to deliver water in ghusl due to hardship, not because it is a creation, so the objection is not rebutted.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents three additional rulings on nullification. First, for a man with a split penis having two heads: if urine exits from the head that is in the normal urethra, wudu is nullified by appearance (zuhur); if from the other head, nullification occurs only when the urine flows beyond the head (sayalan). Second, for a hemorrhoid that protrudes from the anus: if the person manually reinserts it, wudu is nullified because impurity sticks to the hand; if the hemorrhoid re-enters by itself due to sneezing, wudu is not nullified. Similarly, if part of a worm (dood) exits and re-enters, the same ruling applies. Third, regarding urine descent in the urethra: if urine reaches the shaft of the penis (qasabah al-dhakar), it does not nullify wudu; if it reaches the foreskin (qulfah), there is a difference of opinion. The correct view (sahih) is that it does nullify wudu. This ruling is challenged by al-Zayla'i based on the principle that water does not have to reach the foreskin in ghusl for janabah, but this is answered by noting that the correct view in ghusl is that water must reach it, so there is no inconsistency. However, Fath al-Qadir states that the relied-upon (mu'tamad) view in ghusl is that reaching the foreskin is not obligatory due to hardship (haraj), not because it is like the shaft. Thus, the objection from al-Zayla'i remains unresolved according to this later view. The passage thus presents a nuanced ruling with internal Hanafi debate.

Key Terms
القلفة (al-qulfa): The foreskin of the penis, the skin covering the glans.
الباسور (al-basur): Hemorrhoid, a swollen vein in the anal region that may protrude externally.
الشق (al-shiqq): A split or division in the penis resulting in two heads.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wudu if urine descends to the foreskin (qulfa) of the penis?
A) It does not nullify wudu at all
B) There is a difference of opinion, but the correct view is nullification
C) It nullifies only if it reaches the shaft
D) It never nullifies because the foreskin is like the shaft

Key Takeaway
Wudu is nullified when urine reaches the foreskin (sahih view), when a split penis's secondary head flows, and when a hemorrhoid is manually reinserted.

Hanafi Quranic Proof for Nullification by Any Exit of Impurity
Uses Quran 5:6 as proof that any exit of impurity from the body breaks wudu, and discusses a critique from Fath al-Qadir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi derivation from Quran 5:6 that any exit of impurity nullifies wudu, and evaluate the critique from Fath al-Qadir.

Core Idea
The word 'toilet' in Quran 5:6 is a metonymy for hadath; Hanafis apply it to the broadest necessary consequence: any impure exit breaks wudu.

This lesson examines the Hanafi school's primary textual evidence from the Quran for the ruling that any exit of impurity (normal or abnormal) breaks wudu. We will walk through the linguistic argument of kinayah (metonymy) and then consider a critical objection from the later Hanafi text Fath al-Qadir. By the end, you will understand both the strength and the limitations of this proof within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic verse and its literal meaning
The verse says: 'or one of you comes from the toilet (al-gha'it)'. Literally, al-gha'it is a low-lying place used for relieving oneself. Coming from it is a necessary consequence of answering the call of nature.
This sets the context for the metonymy.
2
Understand the Hanafi argument of kinayah
Hanafis argue that 'coming from the toilet' is a kinayah (metonymy) for the state of hadath (ritual impurity). The necessary (al-lazim) is the coming, and the necessitated (al-malzum) is the hadath. The verse mentions the necessary to indicate the necessitated.
Kinayah is a key usul concept used to expand meaning.
3
Apply the principle of a'amm al-lawazim (most general necessary consequences)
When a kinayah is used, it should be understood in the most general sense of the necessary consequences. Therefore, the nullification of wudu applies to any exit of impurity, not just the normal ones from the two passages.
This is why Hanafis say any impure exit breaks wudu.
4
State the practical ruling derived
According to this argument, all impurities exiting the body (e.g., blood from a wound, pus, vomit, etc.) nullify wudu, whether normal or abnormal.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.
5
Consider the critique from Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir objects that the proof is only valid if one intends the most general necessary consequences of 'coming' and 'impure exit'. The critique suggests that the derivation may not be as absolute as claimed.
This shows internal scholarly scrutiny even within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
