And they used as evidence that what exits from the two passages nullifies absolutely the words of Allah: 'or one of you comes from the toilet' (al-Ma'idah: 6), because it is a name for a low place in the earth intended for need. So coming from it is necessary for relieving oneself, so the necessary (the coming from it) was mentioned and the necessitated (the hadath) was intended as metonymy (kinayah). This is extremely clear and careful. And the apparent meaning in Fath al-Qadir is that the necessary is the exit of impurity, and the necessitated is coming from the toilet. And if it is a metonymy for the necessary, then applying it to the most general of necessary consequences is better, taking precaution in matters of worship. So everything that exits from the human body of impurity is a nullifier, whether normal or abnormal. Thus it was a proof against Malik. And it was objected to in Fath al-Qadir that it is valid only if one intends the most general possible consequences for 'coming' and 'the impure exit' etc. The first. Then the commentator says: 'but it should be preferred in it' meaning the preference of obligation in the mufda according to the first meaning (i.e., the one whose passage of urine and feces is one), and similarly on this meaning the statement of recommendation; it is possible that it is not so, reflect. And the commentator says: 'And if his penis has a split' - in al-Khaniyya and al-Tatarkhaniyya, it is 'wound' instead of 'split'. And the commentator says: 'but in Fath al-Qadir etc.' The apparent reason for his not ruling obligation due to hardship is that it is for one who cannot dissolve it [the marriage?], so the first is carried on the case where it is possible, so there is no contradiction between the two statements, as some have mentioned, and the obligation of ghusl is also based on that. And the commentator says: 'absolutely' meaning whether normal or otherwise. And the commentator says: 'whether normal or abnormal' is an explanation of the generality of the necessary (khuruj), i.e., it is not specific to normal. And the commentator says: 'And it is not hidden that...'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalil) derived from the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:6): 'or one of you comes from the toilet' (aw ja'a ahadun minkum min al-gha'it). The argument is that 'al-gha'it' originally means a low ground used for relieving oneself. So 'coming from it' is a necessary consequence of answering the call of nature. The verse uses the necessary (coming) as a metonymy (kinayah) for the necessitated (hadath, i.e., the state of ritual impurity). According to the Hanafi school, since the verse is a metonymy, it should be interpreted in the broadest possible sense, thus any exit of impurity from the body (normal or abnormal) nullifies wudu. This argument is directed against Imam Malik, who presumably restricts nullification to only normal exits. The text then notes that Fath al-Qadir objects to this interpretation, stating that it is valid only if one intends the most general necessary consequences for 'coming' and 'impure exit'. The objection suggests that the proof may not be as absolute as claimed. The passage then transitions to a commentary addressing previous topics (the mufda, the split penis), clarifying that the word 'split' appears as 'wound' in other sources. It also explains that the reason for the alternative view on the mufda (non-obligation) is hardship, and that the two positions can be reconciled by different circumstances. The text ends abruptly, but the core discussion is the Quranic proof and its critique.

Key Terms
الغائط (al-gha'it): Literally a low-lying area used for defecation; by extension, the act of relieving oneself or the place itself.
كناية (kinayah): A metonymy or indirect expression where the necessary consequence is mentioned in place of the intended meaning.
اللوازم (al-lawazim): Necessary consequences; the meanings that necessarily follow from a statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the word 'gha'it' means any impurity
B) Because the phrase is a metonymy (kinayah) for hadath, and should be applied to the most general necessary consequences
C) Because the verse explicitly mentions all types of impurities
D) Because the verse is general in its wording

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the nullification of wudu by any impure exit from Quran 5:6 through the principle of kinayah and a'amm al-lawazim, though Fath al-Qadir offers a qualification.

Definition and Rulings of the Mufadāt Woman
Defining the term 'مفضاة' and its two meanings with legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the two meanings of mufadāt and state the preponderant ruling of obligation (wujub) as well as the two additional marital rulings, applying them only to the first meaning.

Core Idea
The two meanings of mufadāt carry different rulings: only the first meaning imposes wujub and specific marital restrictions.

The term 'mufadāt' refers to a woman whose urinary and anal passages have merged. Hanafi jurists identify two possible meanings: a true fusion into one passage, or a shared opening with separate passages. This distinction is crucial because it determines whether certain purity and marital rulings apply. In this lesson, we will clarify these meanings and their legal consequences as explained in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Read the passage to understand what 'mufadāt' means and note that it concerns a specific female anatomical condition.
The term is derived from the root 'f-d-y' meaning to be merged.
2
Distinguish between the two meanings
The passage outlines two interpretations: the first meaning is a true single passage; the second is a shared opening but separate passages. Determine which meaning is considered the basis for the rulings that follow.
The author states that the preponderant ruling of obligation applies to the first meaning, not the second.
3
Extract the ruling on wudu
Understand that for the first meaning, the obligation (wujub) of wudu is given preponderance, meaning the condition of certainty makes wudu obligatory. For the second meaning, the correct view is that passing wind from the vagina does not nullify wudu.
4
Identify the additional marital rulings
Two additional rulings are given: (1) if such a woman is divorced thrice and marries another, she is not halal for the first husband unless she becomes pregnant; (2) her husband is forbidden from intercourse unless he can enter vaginally without harm. Both rulings are specific to the first meaning.
The reasoning involves the possibility of anal intercourse due to the fused passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Book of Purification. The ruling of certainty makes obligation preponderant, end. But it should be given preponderance in it according to the first meaning; as for the second meaning, no, because the correct view is that it is not nullified by wind exiting from the vagina. Second, it is forbidden for her husband to have intercourse with her unless he can approach her in her vagina without transgression, as in Fath al-Qadir. And both of these should be specific to her according to the first meaning; as for the second meaning, no, as the mentioned reasoning indicates.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling excerpt from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It defines the term 'مفضاة' (mufadāt), referring to a woman whose urinary and anal passages have merged into one. The author identifies two meanings: in the first, the passages are actually one; in the second, they are separate but the opening is shared. The ruling of obligation (وجوب) is given preponderance for the first meaning, not the second, because the correct view is that passing wind from the vagina does not nullify wudu. Two additional rulings apply: (1) if such a woman is divorced thrice and married to another, she is not halal for the first husband until she becomes pregnant, since intercourse might have occurred anally; (2) her husband is forbidden from intercourse unless he can enter vaginally without causing harm. These rulings are specific to the first meaning of mufadāt.

Key Terms
مفضاة (mufadāt): A woman whose urinary and anal passages have merged into one, with two interpretations considered by Hanafi jurists.
سبيلان (sabilān): The two passages: the urethra/urinary tract and the anus/rectum.

The Condition for Blood to Nullify Wudu: Flow vs Swelling
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute on the limit of flow required for nullification of wudu by blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for blood nullifying wudu and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the limit of flow.

Core Idea
Wudu is nullified only when blood flows to a place requiring purification; mere swelling does not suffice per the relied-upon view.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common issue in purification: when does blood from a wound nullify wudu? The general principle is that impurity nullifies only if it emerges and reaches a place that must be cleansed. However, scholars differed on what constitutes 'reaching'—is actual flow necessary, or does swelling at the wound suffice? This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the emergence of blood from a wound. Determine whether wudu is nullified (naqd) or remains valid.
2
State the general condition for nullification
According to the Hanafi school, nullification occurs when impurity reaches a place subject to obligatory or recommended purification. Flow (sayalaan) is the key criterion.
3
Outline the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf: nullification requires that blood rises and descends (i.e., flows) on the surface. Muhammad: nullification occurs when blood swells at the wound and becomes larger than the wound head, even without flow.
Zufar adds a further condition for nasal blood: it must reach the soft part of the nose.
4
Analyze the evidence and reasoning
The majority (Abu Yusuf) argue that flow is necessary because without flow, the blood is still effectively internal. Muhammad considers swelling as sufficient indication of exit. The author weighs the views.
Al-Sarakhsi and al-Diraya preferred Muhammad's view, but the majority (al-Hidayah, Fath al-Qadir) prefer Abu Yusuf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon view is that of Abu Yusuf: flow is required. If blood swells but does not flow, wudu is not nullified.
The practical difference is whether one must repeat wudu in cases of swelling without flow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
صماخ الأذن خرقها وليس ذلك إلا لكونه يندب تطهيره في الغسل ونحوه. وكذا إذا اقتصد وخرج دم كثير وسال بحيث لم يتلطخ رأس الجرح فإنه ينقض الوضوء لكونه وصل إلى ثوب أو مكان يلحقهما حكم التطهير فتنبه لهذا فإنه يدفع كلام كثير من الشارحين، ولذا قال في فتح القدير : لو خرج من جرح في العين دم فسال إلى الجانب الآخر منها لا ينقض لأنه لا يلحقه حكم هو وجوب التطهير أو ندبه. فقول بعضهم المراد أن يصل إلى موضع تجب طهارته محمول على أن المراد بالوجوب الثبوت وقول الحدّادي إذا نزل الدم إلى قصبة الأنف لا ينقض محمول على أنه لم يصل إلى ما يسنّ إيصال الماء إليه في الاستنشاق فهو في حكم الباطن حينئذ توفيقاً بين العبارات، وقول من قال «إذا نزل الدم إلى مالان من الأنف نقض لا يقتضي عدم النقض إذا وصل إلى ما اشتدّ منه لا بالمفهوم والصريح بخلافه، وقد أوضحه في غاية البيان والعناية والمراد بالوصول المذكور سيلانه .
واختلف في حده ففي المحيط حده أن يعلو وينحدر عن أبي يوسف وعن محمد إذا انتفخ على رأس الجرح وصار أكبر من رأسه نقض، والصحيح الأول. وفي الدراية جعل قول محمد أصح واختاره السرخسي، وفي فتح القدير أنه الأولى، وفي مبسوط شيخ الإسلام تورم رأس الجرح فظهر به قيح ونحوه لا ينقض ما لم يجاوز الورم لأنه لا يجب غسل موضع الورم
نهر
الزيادة وأنه يتعين حمل الوجوب على الثبوت فتدبر منصفاً . قوله : ( بحيث لم يتلطخ رأس الجرح) أي لم يتجاوز إلى محل يلحقه التطهير من البدن وإنما قيد به ليبين أنه غير مقيد بالبدن بناء على ما ذكره من التعميم السابق، وفيه أنه يقتضي والحالة هذه أنه لو سال إلى غير ما ذكر كبحر أو مثلاً أو عذرة أو غير ذلك لا ينتقض وهو باطل فكان الظاهر إبدال قوله أولاً فناقض بشرط أن يصل الخ» بقولنا بشرط أن يسيل ظاهر البدن أو يصل إلى موضع تجب طهارته أو تندب من البدن فيدخل في شرط السيلان ظاهر البدن مسألة الاقتصاد حيث لم يتلطخ رأس الجرح، ومسألة الأنف والأذن مما سال داخله تدخل في قولنا أو يصل الخ ولا يرد عليه ما مر فتدبر قوله : (وقد أوضحه في غاية البيان والعناية قال في غاية البيان: قوله إلى مالان . من الأنف أي إلى المارن «وما» بمعنى الذي. فإن قلت: لم قيد بهذا القيد مع أن الرواية مسطورة في الكتب عن أصحابنا أن الدم إذا نزل إلى قصبة الأنف ينقض الوضوء ولا حاجة إلى أن ينزل إلى ما لان من الأنف فأي فائدة في هذا القيد إذن سوى التكرار بلا فائدة لأن هذا الحكم قد علم في أول الفصل من قوله والدم والقيح إذا خرجا من البدن فتجاوزا إلى موضع يلحقه حكم التطهير؟ قلت بياناً لاتفاق أصحابنا جميعاً لأن عند زفر لا ينتقض الوضوء ما لم ينزل الدم إلى مالان الأنف من لعدم الظهور قبل ذلك اهـ . وهو شاهد قوي على ما قاله فلا تغتر بتزييف صاحب النهر والله تعالى ولي التوفيق قوله : واختاره السرخسي) عبارة الفتح بعد نقله عبارة الدراية واختار السرخسي الأول وهو أولى اهـ والأول في عبارة الفتح هو قول أبي يوسف، وكذا ذكر في الدراية قوله أولاً ثم ذكر قول محمد ثانياً ثم قال والصحيح الأول فليراجع.
The Book of Purity. The ear: its piercing is only because it is recommended to clean it in ghusl and the like. Similarly, if it [the blood] is moderate and a lot of blood comes out and flows such that the head of the wound is not smeared, then it nullifies wudu because it reached a garment or place that is subject to the ruling of purification. So pay attention to this, for it repels the speech of many commentators. Hence, it is said in Fath al-Qadir: If blood comes out of a wound in the eye and flows to the other side of it, it does not nullify [wudu] because it is not subject to the ruling of obligatory or recommended purification. So the statement of some scholars that the meaning is that it reaches a place whose purification is obligatory is interpreted to mean that the intent of 'obligatory' is 'established'. And the statement of al-Haddadi that if blood descends to the nasal cavity it does not nullify is interpreted to mean that it did not reach what is recommended to bring water to in sniffing, so it is in the ruling of the interior at that time, reconciling the expressions. And the statement of whoever said 'if blood descends to the soft part of the nose, it nullifies' does not necessitate non-nullification if it reaches the hard part, neither by implication nor explicitly; rather the opposite. And he clarified it in Ghayat al-Bayan wa al-'Inayah, and the meaning of the mentioned 'reaching' is its flow. And there is disagreement on its limit. In al-Muhit: its limit is that it rises and descends — from Abu Yusuf. And from Muhammad: if it swells at the head of the wound and becomes larger than the head, it nullifies. The first is sound. In al-Diraya, he made Muhammad's statement more correct, and al-Sarakhsi chose it. In Fath al-Qadir, it is preferred. In the Mabsoot of Shaykh al-Islam: swelling of the head of the wound with pus and the like appearing does not nullify as long as it does not exceed the swelling, because washing the place of swelling is not obligatory. ... [translation continues] His statement: (such that the head of the wound is not smeared) i.e., it did not exceed to a place subject to purifi' from the body; he restricted it to clarify that it is not restricted to the body based on the previous generalization. It implies in this case that if it flowed to other than what was mentioned, like a sea or impurity or something else, it does not nullify, which is false. So the apparent [solution] is to replace his earlier statement 'it nullifies on condition that it reaches...' with our statement: 'on condition that it flows on the apparent [skin] of the body or reaches a place whose purification is obligatory or recommended from the body.' Thus enters under the condition of flow on the apparent body the case of moderation where the head of the wound is not smeared, and the case of the nose and ear where it flows inside enters under our statement 'or reaches...' and the earlier passage does not refute this. His statement: (and he clarified it in Ghayat al-Bayan wa al-'Inayah). He said in Ghayat al-Bayan: his statement 'to the soft part of the nose' means to the fleshy part, and 'ma' is the relative pronoun. If you say: why restrict with this restriction when the narration is recorded in the books from our Companions that blood descending to the nasal cavity nullifies wudu, and there is no need for it to descend to the soft part of the nose? So what benefit is there in this restriction besides repetition? Because this ruling is already known from the beginning of the chapter from his statement 'blood and pus when they exit the body and reach a place subject to the ruling of purification.' I reply: to clarify the agreement of all our Companions, because according to Zufar, wudu is not nullified until the blood descends to the soft part of the nose due to lack of manifestation before that. This is a strong evidence for what he said, so do not be deceived by the refutation of the author of al-Nahr. And Allah is the granter of success. His statement: (and al-Sarakhsi chose it). The expression in al-Fath after quoting the expression of al-Diraya: 'al-Sarakhsi chose the first, and it is more appropriate.' The first in the expression of al-Fath is the statement of Abu Yusuf. And it is mentioned in al-Diraya: he mentioned the first, then mentioned the statement of Muhammad, then said 'the first is sound.' So it should be referenced.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the nullification of wudu by blood that flows from a wound. The core principle: blood nullifies wudu only if it reaches a place that requires purification (either obligatory or recommended). The author begins by discussing the ear: its piercing is recommended to be cleaned during ghusl, implying it is an external part. If blood flows from a wound but does not soil the wound itself, it still nullifies wudu because it reaches a garment or spot that must be purified. This refutes some commentators who thought otherwise. The text then cites Fath al-Qadir regarding a wound in the eye: blood flowing to the other side of the eye does not nullify wudu because that area is not subject to obligatory or recommended cleansing. The term 'obligatory' in this context is interpreted as 'established' (thubut). For the nose, al-Haddadi's view that blood descending to the nasal cavity (قصبة الأنف) does not nullify is reconciled by saying it has not reached the area that is recommended to be rinsed during istinshaq, so it is still considered internal. The text clarifies that the correct condition for nullification is that the blood actually flows (سيلان), not merely appears. It also mentions that Zufar requires the blood to reach the soft part of the nose (الما رن) for nullification to occur. The passage then addresses a dispute on the limit (حد) of flow: Abu Yusuf says the limit is that the blood rises and descends (i.e., flows), while Muhammad says nullification occurs when the blood swells at the wound and becomes larger than the head of the wound. The sounder view is that of Abu Yusuf, though al-Sarakhsi preferred Muhammad's view. The commentary then examines the phrase 'such that the head of the wound is not smeared' and notes that this condition is not restricted to the body alone; if blood flows to something else like a sea or impurity, it still nullifies. The author suggests rephrasing the condition to avoid confusion: wudu is nullified if blood flows on the apparent skin or reaches a place whose purification is obligatory or recommended. The passage also explains why the restriction in the Ghayat al-Bayan regarding the nose is useful: it shows the agreement of the Hanafi school against Zufar. Finally, it confirms that the first view (Abu Yusuf's) is the correct one and warns against being misled by the refutation in al-Nahr.

Key Terms
صماخ (simaakh): The ear's internal canal or the external opening of the ear; specifically, 'صماخ الأذن' refers to the ear canal, which is considered an external part of the body that requires cleaning in ghusl.
نقض (naqd): To nullify or invalidate, specifically the nullification of wudu (ritual ablution) due to the emergence of impurity (hadath).
سيلان (sayalaan): The flow of liquid (blood, pus, etc.) from a wound; the condition for nullification according to the relied-upon view is actual flow, not mere swelling or appearance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what condition must be met for blood from a wound to nullify wudu?
A) The blood must swell at the wound and become larger than the wound head.
B) The blood must flow (سيلان) to a place subject to purification.
C) The blood must soil the head of the wound.
D) The wound must be in an external organ.

Key Takeaway
Wudu is nullified by blood only when it flows to a place requiring purification; mere swelling without flow does not nullify wudu in the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Nullification by Pus, Blisters, Eye Discharge, and Blood from Wounds
Detailed fiqh rulings on nullification by bodily discharges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on nullification of wudu by pus, ichor, blister fluid, eye discharge, and blood from wounds, and apply the criterion of flowing versus non-flowing and probability of impurity to determine nullification.

Core Idea
Pus and ichor always nullify wudu if they flow; blisters only if originally blood; eye discharge nullifies only when impurity is probable; blood nullifies if it would have flowed without intervention.

In this lesson, we examine the detailed rulings on what nullifies wudu from various bodily impurities. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes between different types of discharges—pus, ichor, blister fluid, eye discharge, and blood—and provides criteria based on origin, flow, and probability. Understanding these distinctions is essential for maintaining purity in daily prayers and worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of impurity
First, determine what kind of bodily discharge is involved: pus (qayḥ), ichor (ṣadīd), blister fluid (nafṭa), eye discharge (ramad/amash tears), or blood from a wound. Each has specific rules.
Pus and ichor always come from an ailment; blister fluid may be blood or water; eye discharge may be pure tears or pus; blood from wounds varies.
2
Determine if the impurity flows or would flow
The key criterion for nullification is whether the impurity flows from its place and reaches a point that requires purification. If it does not flow (e.g., stays in place or is absorbed), wudu is not nullified. For blood from a wound, consider if it would have flowed had it not been wiped or treated.
The text distinguishes between cases where wiping or dust causes more blood that would have flowed if left alone.
3
Apply the ruling for blisters
For a blister (nafṭa), check whether the fluid originally came from blood or is simply water. If it was originally blood, it nullifies; if water, it does not. This is determined by the nature of the blister.
The author supports the view that blister fluid derived from blood is impure and nullifies wudu.
4
Handle eye discharge by assessing probability
For chronic eye conditions (ramad/amash) causing tearing, if there is doubt whether the tears contain pus/ichor, the default ruling is that wudu is not nullified (certainty is not removed by doubt). Renewing wudu is recommended (mustahabb). However, if there is overwhelming probability (غلبنة الظن) that the discharge is impure, based on medical opinion or signs, it becomes obligatory.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; some scholars like those of Fath al-Qadir and al-Mujtaba hold it obligatory even with strong probability.
5
Consider blood from wounds with intervention
If blood is in a wound and the person wipes it with a cloth, or a fly consumes it, causing more blood to appear, evaluate whether that additional blood would have flowed if not wiped. If it would have flowed, wudu is nullified; otherwise, it is not. Similarly, if dust or ash is thrown on the wound and blood reappears multiple times in one session, all appearances are considered cumulatively (yujma‘) as if they were one continuous flow.
If the appearances occur in separate sessions, they are not combined.
6
Understand the bandaged wound ruling
If a wound is bandaged and moisture penetrates the inner lining but does not reach the outside, it does not nullify wudu. The nullification only occurs if the moisture reaches a place requiring purification.
This is based on the principle that only external flow matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
ذلك.
عن
فلم يتجاوزه إلى موضع يلحقه التطهير، ثم الجرح والنفطة وماء السرة والثدي والأذن والعين إذا كان لعلة سواء على الأصح. وعن الحسن أن ماء النفطة لا ينقض . قال الحلواني : وفيه توسعة لمن به جرب أو جدري كذا في المعراج وفي التبيين والقيح : الخارج من الأذن أو الصديد إن كان بدون الوجع لا ينقض ومع الوجع ينقض لأنه دليل الجرح روى الحلواني ا هـ . وفيه نظر بل الظاهر إذا كان الخارج قيحاً أو صديداً ينقض سواء كان مع وجع أو بدونه لأنهما لا يخرجان إلا عن علة، نعم هذا التفصيل حسن فيما إذا كان الخارج ماء ليس غير. وفيه أيضاً ولو كان في عينيه رمد أو عمش يسيل منهما الدموع - قالوا - يؤمر بالوضوء لوقت كل صلاة لاحتمال أن يكون صديداً أو قيحاً ! أنه اهـ. وهذا التعليل يقتضي أمر استحباب فإن الشك والاحتمال في كونه ناقضاً لا يوجب الحكم بالنقض إذ اليقين يزول بالشك، نعم إذا علم من طريق غلبة الظن بأخبار الأطباء أو بعلامات تغلب على ظنّ المبتلي يجب. ولو كان الدم في الجرح فأخذه بخرقة أو أكله الذباب فازداد في مكانه، فإن كان بحيث يزيد ويسيل لو لم يأخذه بنفسه بطل وضوءه وإلا فلا. وكذلك إذا ألقى عليه تراب أو رماد ثم ظهر ثانياً وتربه ثم وثم فهو كذلك يجمع كله. قال في الذخيرة قالوا : وإنما يجمع إذا كان في مجلس واحد مرة بعد أخرى، أما إذا كان في مجالس مختلفة لا يجمع. ولو ربط الجرح فنفذت البلة إلى طاق لا إلى الخارج نقض قال في فتح القدير ويجب أن يكون معناه إذا كان
وقد
من
قوله : ( والنفطة) هي القرحة التي امتلأت وحان قشرها وهي من قولهم انتفط فلان إذا امتلأ غضباً. قال في الجمهرة: تنفطت يد الرجل إذا رق جلدها من العمل وصار فيها كالماء، والكف نفيطة ومنفوطة. كذا في غاية البيان وقال أيضاً بعده هذا أي النقض إذا كانت النفطة أصلها دماً . تكون من الابتداء ماء. قوله: (نعم هذا التفصيل حسن الخ) قال بعض الأفاضل : فيه أن الماء . فروع الدم كما قاله الزيلعي لأنه ينضح فيصير صديداً قوله : (وهذا التعليل يقتضي أنه أمر استحباب الخ) رده في النهر بأن الأمر للوجوب حقيقة وهذا الاحتمال راجح ، وبأن في فتح القدير صرح بالوجوب وكذا في المجتبى . قال : يجب عليه الوضوء والناس عنه غافلون قوله : (فهو كذلك يجمع كله أقول : التشبيه غير ظاهر إذ ما قبله ليس فيه جمع بل النظر في أنه لو لم يأخذه لسال بنفسه وبينهما فرق ظاهر، فإن الخارج إذا ترك ربما لا يسيل لانسداد المخرج بما خرج فإذا مسحه وخرج غيره مما لا يسيل وفعل ذلك مراراً لا ينتقض وضوءه مع أن ذلك الممسوح في كل مرة إذا جمع يكون سائلاً، وأما هذا فيقتضي النقض بذلك وبينهما منافاة ظاهرة. وانظر ما الفرق بين ما إذا أخذه بخرقة أو ألقى عليه تراباً حيث يجمع في الثانية دون الأولى، ثم ظهر أن المراد بالجمع هو النظر فيه لو ترك. قال في التتارخانية : يجمع جميع ما نشف فلو كان بحيث لو تركه سال جعل حدثاً وإنما يعرف ذلك بالاجتهاد وغالب الظن. قوله : (ولو ربط الجرح إلى آخر كلامه أقول : يفهم من هذا حكم ماء
ربما
The Book of Purity. That. From [the previous]? Then the wound, the blister, the water of the navel, the breast, the ear, and the eye — if there is a disease, it is the same according to the most correct view. And from al-Hasan: that the water of the blister does not nullify. Al-Halwani said: In it is a concession for one who has scabies or smallpox. Likewise in al-Miraj and al-Tabyin. And the pus: what comes out of the ear or the ichor, if it is without pain, it does not nullify; if with pain, it nullifies because it indicates a wound. Narrated by al-Halwani. There is consideration in this; rather, the apparent view is that if what comes out is pus or ichor, it nullifies whether with pain or without, because they only come out from an ailment. Yes, this detail is good when what comes out is water, not otherwise. Also, if he has ophthalmia (رمد) or chronic watering (عمش) in his eyes such that tears flow from them — they said: he is ordered to perform wudu for the time of each prayer because of the possibility that it might be ichor or pus. This reasoning indicates that it is a recommendation, because doubt and possibility regarding its being a nullifier do not necessitate the ruling of nullification, since certainty is not removed by doubt. Yes, if he knows through overwhelming probability (غلبة الظن) from the reports of doctors or through signs that prevail in the opinion of the afflicted, then it is obligatory. And if there is blood in the wound and he takes it with a cloth or a fly eats it, and it increases in its place, then if it is such that it would increase and flow if he had not taken it himself, his wudu becomes invalid; otherwise no. Similarly, if he throws dust or ash on it, then it appears a second time with its dust, then again, then again, then it is gathered together. It is said in al-Dhakhira: they said: it is only gathered if it is in one session repeatedly; as for different sessions, it is not gathered. And if he bandaged the wound and the moisture penetrates to the lining but not to the outside, it nullifies. In Fath al-Qadir: it must be understood that it is when... His statement: (and the blister [النفطة]) is the sore that fills up and is about to peel; it is from their saying 'intafata' so-and-so when he is filled with anger. It is said in al-Jamhara: a man's palm blistered when its skin became thin from work and there is water in it. Similarly in Ghayat al-Bayan, and he also said after this: 'this nullification is when the blister originally was blood; it is from the beginning water.' His statement: (Yes, this detail is good...) Some virtuous [scholars] said: that water is a branch of blood, as al-Zayla'i said, because it oozes and becomes ichor. His statement: (This reasoning indicates that it is a recommendation...) It is refuted in al-Nahr by saying that the command is essentially for obligation, and this probability is preponderant, and because in Fath al-Qadir he explicitly stated obligation, and likewise in al-Mujtaba. He said: he must perform wudu, and people are heedless of it. His statement: (Similarly, it is gathered — I say: the comparison is not apparent, because what precedes it does not involve gathering; rather, it is about the consideration that if he had not taken it, it would have flowed by itself. There is a clear difference between them, because the discharge if left may not flow due to blockage of the exit by what came out; if he wipes it and something else comes out that does not flow, and he does that repeatedly, his wudu is not nullified, even though that which is wiped each time, if gathered, would be flowing. And this [the previous case] necessitates nullification by that, and there is an apparent contradiction between them. So consider the difference between when he takes it with a cloth and when he throws dust on it, where gathering applies in the second but not the first. Then it became clear that the meaning of gathering is considering it as if left. It is said in al-Tatarkhaniya: all that which is absorbed is gathered. So if it is such that if he had left it it would have flowed, it is considered a hadath (event). This is known by ijtihad and predominant probability. His statement: (And if he bandaged the wound...) to the end of his speech. I say: from this, the ruling of water is understood. Perhaps...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on nullification of wudu by various impurities. The text begins by stating that for wounds, blisters, water from navel, breast, ear, and eye, if there is a disease (علة), the ruling is the same (i.e., it nullifies if it reaches a place requiring purification). It then reports a view from Imam al-Hasan that the water of a blister does not nullify, but Al-Halwani says this provides leniency for those with scabies or smallpox. Regarding pus (قيح) and ichor (صديد), there is a reported distinction: if without pain, it does not nullify; if with pain, it does, because pain indicates a wound. The author rejects this, stating that pus and ichor only come from an ailment, so they always nullify. However, this distinction is acceptable if the discharge is just water. The text then addresses eye diseases like ophthalmia (رمد) where tears flow. Some scholars said the person is ordered to perform wudu for each prayer time because the tears might be pus or ichor. The author analyzes this: the reasoning suggests it is recommended (istihbab), not obligatory, because doubt doesn't remove certainty. However, if there is strong probability (غلبة الظن) from doctors or signs, it becomes obligatory. The text then addresses a case where blood is in the wound and he wipes it with a cloth or a fly eats it, causing more blood to appear. If the blood would have flowed had he not wiped it, his wudu is invalidated; otherwise not. Similarly, if he throws dust or ash and it reappears, it is all considered together (يجمع). The condition is that it happens in one session; if in different sessions, it is not aggregated. The text also discusses a bandaged wound: if moisture penetrates to the inner lining but not outside, it nullifies wudu, according to Fath al-Qadir, provided the moisture reaches a place requiring purification. The commentary then examines the definition of 'blister' (نفطة) and explains that the nullification depends on whether the blister originally contained blood or water. If it was blood, it nullifies; if water, it may not. The author also critiques a comparison made by a previous commentator, clarifying that the ruling of gathering applies only when considering the blood as if it had been left to flow. Finally, the text from al-Tatarkhaniya explains that absorbed moisture is considered as gathered, and determination is based on ijtihad and predominant probability.

Key Terms
نفطة (nafṭa): A blister on the skin that is filled with fluid and about to peel. The ruling on nullification depends on whether the fluid originated as blood (then nullifies) or is mere water (then does not).
قيح (qayḥ): Pus that exits from a wound or abscess. According to the relied-upon view, it always nullifies wudu if it flows, regardless of pain.
استحباب (istiḥbāb): Recommendation; an act that is encouraged but not obligatory. In this context, renewing wudu for each prayer due to eye discharge is considered recommended (mustahabb), not wajib.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Predominant probability; a degree of certainty short of absolute certainty but sufficient to establish a ruling in the absence of yaqin. In this lesson, it is the criterion that elevates eye discharge renewal from mustahabb to wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the text, when does the fluid from a blister nullify wudu?
A) Always, because it comes from a wound.
B) Only if it flows and reaches a place requiring purification, regardless of its origin.
C) Only if the blister's content originally came from blood.
D) Only if it causes pain.

Key Takeaway
Nullification of wudu by bodily discharges hinges on whether the impurity flows from the body and reaches a place requiring purification, with special rules for blisters (origin of fluid) and eye discharge (probability of impurity).

Rulings on Wudu Nullification by Wound Discharges, Blisters, and Tears
Expansion on wudu nullification by bodily discharges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nullification by pus, ichor, blister water, and tears from diseased eyes, and explain the commentator's critique of the pain-based distinction.

Core Idea
Pus and ichor always nullify wudu; the pain-based distinction applies only to watery discharge.

This passage expands on the Hanafi rulings regarding what nullifies wudu. It covers wounds, blisters, moisture from navel/breast/ear/eye, pus, ichor, and tears from diseased eyes. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) engages with earlier views, providing a nuanced analysis that distinguishes between different types of discharges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the discharge types
List the various discharges discussed: wound moisture, blister water, pus, ichor, tears from ophthalmia/watery eyes. Note each is a potential nullifier of wudu.
The passage groups these under the general category of 'what comes out' with specific rulings.
2
State the general rule for blisters and wounds
The soundest view (al-asahh) is that water from a blister, wound, navel, breast, ear, or eye nullifies if due to a cause (illness). Otherwise, if it does not flow, it may not nullify.
The key condition is the presence of a 'cause' (illa) indicating the discharge is impure.
3
Analyze the exception and the commentator's criticism
Al-Halwani reports that pus/ichor without pain does not nullify; with pain it does. Ibn Nujaym criticizes this, stating that pus/ichor always nullify because they only come from an ailment. He concedes the distinction applies only to pure water.
The argument is talil (reasoning): the illa of 'coming from an ailment' is constant, so pain is irrelevant.
4
Apply the reasoning to tears from diseased eyes
Tears from ophthalmia might be ichor. Scholars command wudu for each prayer out of caution. The commentator clarifies this is istihbab (recommended), not wajib, because doubt does not remove certainty. Only if overwhelming probability indicates pus does it become obligatory.
Epistemological principle: certainty (yaqin) is not removed by doubt (shakk).
5
Synthesize the overall ruling
Combine the findings: for any discharge, if it is certainly from a wound/ailment (like pus/ichor), it nullifies. If uncertain, wudu remains valid unless overwhelming probability indicates impurity.
This protects the validity of wudu while accommodating medical reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك. عن فلم يتجاوزه إلى موضع يلحقه التطهير، ثم الجرح والنفطة وماء السرة والثدي والأذن والعين إذا كان لعلة سواء على الأصح. وعن الحسن أن ماء النفطة لا ينقض . قال الحلواني : وفيه توسعة لمن به جرب أو جدري كذا في المعراج وفي التبيين والقيح : الخارج من الأذن أو الصديد إن كان بدون الوجع لا ينقض ومع الوجع ينقض لأنه دليل الجرح روى الحلواني ا هـ . وفيه نظر بل الظاهر إذا كان الخارج قيحاً أو صديداً ينقض سواء كان مع وجع أو بدونه لأنهما لا يخرجان إلا عن علة، نعم هذا التفصيل حسن فيما إذا كان الخارج ماء ليس غير. وفيه أيضاً ولو كان في عينيه رمد أو عمش يسيل منهما الدموع - قالوا - يؤمر بالوضوء لوقت كل صلاة لاحتمال أن يكون صديداً أو قيحاً ! أنه اهـ. وهذا التعليل يقتضي أمر استحباب فإن الشك والاحتمال في كونه ناقضاً لا يوجب الحكم بالنقض إذ اليقين يزول بالشك، نعم إذا علم من طريق غلبة الظن بأخبار الأطباء أو بعلامات تغلب على ظنّ المبتلي يجب.
That. He did not exceed it to a place that requires purification, then the wound and the blister and water of the navel, breast, ear, and eye if due to a cause are the same according to the soundest view. And from al-Hasan that the water of the blister does not nullify. al-Halwani said: In it is leniency for one with scabies or smallpox. Likewise in al-Mi'raj and al-Tabyeen. And pus: what comes out of the ear or ichor: if without pain it does not nullify, and with pain it nullifies because it is evidence of a wound, narrated by al-Halwani. There is consideration in it, rather apparently if what comes out is pus or ichor, it nullifies whether with pain or without because they only come out due to an ailment. Yes, this detail is good when what comes out is water only. And also: if in his eyes there is ophthalmia or watery eyes, tears flow from them—they said: he is commanded to perform wudu for the time of every prayer due to the possibility that it is ichor or pus. That is it. And this reasoning implies that it is a matter of recommendation, for doubt and possibility about it being a nullifier do not necessitate the ruling of nullification, since certainty is not removed by doubt. Yes, if it is known by way of overwhelming probability from the reports of physicians or by signs that overcome the afflicted person's probability, it becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings of what nullifies wudu. The author begins by stating that if moisture from a wound or blister does not exceed a point that would require purification (i.e., it does not flow), the ruling is that it does not nullify. However, for wounds, blisters, and water from the navel, breast, ear, and eye, if they are due to a cause (like illness), they nullify wudu on the soundest view. This is a general rule. Then a different opinion from Hasan al-Basri is cited: water from a blister does not nullify. Al-Halwani notes this provides leniency for those with scabies or smallpox. Next, the text discusses pus and ichor from the ear: according to a narration from al-Halwani, if there is no pain, it does not nullify; if there is pain, it does. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) critiques this, stating that pus and ichor always nullify regardless of pain because they only come from an ailment. He concedes the distinction applies only if the discharge is mere water. Then the passage addresses tears from eyes with ophthalmia: scholars say wudu is commanded for each prayer because the tears might be ichor or pus. The commentator points out this reasoning implies recommendation (istihbab), not obligation, because doubt does not remove certainty of wudu. However, if one gains overwhelming probability (through physicians or signs) that the tears are actually pus, then wudu becomes obligatory.

Key Terms
النفطة (al-nafṭah): A blister that has filled and is about to peel off; its water is discussed regarding wudu nullification.
القيح (al-qayḥ): Pus that comes out of a wound or ear; according to the sound view, it always nullifies wudu.
الصديد (al-ṣadīd): Ichor or watery discharge from a wound; ruling same as pus.
الرمد (al-ramad): Ophthalmia; inflammation of the eye causing discharge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's final position, what is the ruling on pus discharging from a wound without pain?
A) It does not nullify wudu.
B) It nullifies wudu because it comes from an ailment.
C) It nullifies only if it flows beyond the wound.
D) It nullifies only if the person feels pain.

Key Takeaway
Pus and ichor always nullify wudu; the pain-based distinction is invalid for these discharges, and certainty of wudu prevails over doubt.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Nullification of Wudu by Blood and Wound Moisture: The Flow Test
Rulings on blood wiped or eaten by flies, combining, and moisture from a bandaged wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi flow test to determine if blood or wound moisture nullifies wudu, and state the mufta bihi on squeezed blood.

Core Idea
Only flowing impurity nullifies wudu; if it would not flow naturally, it is not a nullifier.

This lesson examines the detailed Hanafi rulings on when blood, pus, or blister fluid from a wound nullifies wudu. The key is the 'flow test': if the discharge would have flowed when left alone, it nullifies; otherwise not. We also explore the dispute on intentionally squeezed blood and the relied-upon position, along with the key evidence from the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaish.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Flow Test
For any discharge from a wound, ask: 'Would it have flowed (i.e., moved beyond the wound site) if left untouched?' If yes, it nullifies wudu; if no, it does not. This principle governs all cases.
The test is objective, not based on amount or color.
2
Apply to Cases in the Text
Apply the test to blood wiped by a cloth, eaten by flies, or covered with dust/ash. If the blood would have flowed without intervention, it nullifies. If not, it does not. The total moisture is combined only within one sitting.
Combining requires a single majlis (sitting).
3
Distinguish Special Cases
Blister water (حمصة): if it flows by itself, it nullifies and is impure; if it does not flow, it is pure. For a tick or leech: small size – no nullification; large size – nullification because blood inside is flowing.
The size of the tick indicates whether blood would flow if the tick were removed.
4
Analyze the Squeezing Dispute
If blood is squeezed out and would not have flowed without squeezing, the majority (Sahib al-Hidayah) say it does not nullify. Shams al-A'imma says it does, likening it to phlebotomy. The mufta bihi is the former.
The dispute hinges on whether extraction is considered in the hukm.
5
Identify the Dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaish (Bukhari), where the Prophet distinguished between menstrual blood and a vein. This indicates that not all blood nullifies; analogy is used for other discharges.
The hadith is ahad, but accepted as evidence for this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الدم في الجرح فأخذه بخرقة أو أكله الذباب فازداد في مكانه، فإن كان بحيث يزيد ويسيل لو لم يأخذه بنفسه بطل وضوءه وإلا فلا. وكذلك إذا ألقى عليه تراب أو رماد ثم ظهر ثانياً وتربه ثم وثم فهو كذلك يجمع كله. قال في الذخيرة قالوا : وإنما يجمع إذا كان في مجلس واحد مرة بعد أخرى، أما إذا كان في مجالس مختلفة لا يجمع. ولو ربط الجرح فنفذت البلة إلى طاق لا إلى الخارج نقض قال في فتح القدير ويجب أن يكون معناه إذا كان بحيث لولا الرباط سال لأن القميص لو تردد على الجرح فابتل لا ينجس ما لم يكن كذلك لأنه ليس بحدث. وفي المحيط : مص القراد فامتلأ إن كان صغيراً لا ينقض كما لو مص الذباب وإن كان كبيراً نقض كمص العلقة ا.هـ. وعللوه بأن الدم في الكبير يكون سائلاً قالوا : ولا ينقض ما ظهر من موضعه ولم يرتق كالنفظة إذا قشرت ولا ما ارتقى عن موضعه ولم يسل كالدم المرتقى من مغرز الإبرة والحاصل في الخلال من الأسنان، وفي الخبز من العض، وفي الأصبع من إدخاله في الأنف. وفي منية المصلي: ولو استنثر فسقطت دم لم تنقض وضوءه وإن قطرت قطرة دم انتقض ا هـ . . وأما ما سال بعصر وكان بحيث لو لم يعصر لم يسل قالوا لا ينقض لأنه ليس بخارج كتلة من أنفه وإنما هو مخرج وهو مختار صاحب الهداية، وقال شمس الأئمة ينقض وهو حدث عمد عنده وهو الأصح كذا في فتح القدير معزياً إلى الكافي لأنه لا تأثير يظهر للإخراج وعدمه في هذا الحكم بل لكونه خارجاً نجساً، وذلك يتحقق مع الاخراج كما يتحقق مع عدمه فصار كالفصد، كيف وجميع الأدلة الموردة من السنة والقياس يفيد تعليق النقض بالخارج النجس وهو ثابت في المخرج اهـ. وضعفه في العناية بأن الإخراج ليس بمنصوص عليه وإن كان يستلزمه فكان ثبوته غير قصدي ولا معتبر به ا هـ. وهذا كله مذهبنا واستدلوا له بأحاديث ضعفها في فتح القدير وأحسن ما يستدل به حديث فاطمة والقياس. أما الأول فما رواه البخاري عن عائشة جاءت فاطمة بنت أبي حبيش إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت : يا رسول الله إني امرأة استحاض فلا أطهر أفأدع الصلاة؟ قال : لا إنما ذلك عرق وليست بالحيضة، فإذا الحمصة لو نفذ إلى الرباط، ويقيد بما قيده به في الفتح، فالحكم فيها مع السيلان وعدمه فما ليس له قوة السيلان إذا أصاب الثوب منه ولو كان في محال كثيرة لا ينجس لأن المحل المصاب لا يصل منه إليه إلا بلل غير سائل وهو طاهر، وكذا باقي المحل. وكذلك إذا أصاب مائعاً لا ينجسه على الصحيح وهذه مسألة عمت بها البلوى وكثر السؤال عنها وللشرنبلالي فيها رسالة لا بأس بذكر حاصلها وذلك أنه قال بعد سرد النقول : فبهذا علمت أن ماء الحمصة الذي لا يسيل بقوة نفسه طاهرة لا ينقض الوضوء ولا ينجس الثوب ولا الخرقة الموضوعة عليه ولا الماء إذا أصابه، فلو كان له قوة السيلان بنفسه يكون ذلك السائل الخارج نجساً ناقضاً للوضوء، ويلزم غسل ما أصابه من الثوب، ولا يجوز لصاحبه الصلاة حال سيلانه فإنه ناقض للوضوء نجس ولا يصير به صاحب عذر لأنه هو الذي لا يقدر على رد عذره ولو بالربط والحشو الذي يمنع خروج النجس. وصاحب الحمصة التي يسيل الخارج منها بوضعها إذا ترك الوضع لا يبقى بالمحل شيء يسيل فلا يتصوّر له طهارة ولا صحة صلاة حينئذ لقدرته على المنع بترك الوضع، فلا يبقى له مخلص مع الوضع والسيلان إلا بالتقليد مع مراعاة الشروط في مذهب من قلده احترازاً عن التلفيق الباطل. هذا حاصل ما ذكره رحمه الله تعالى. قوله : (وضعفه في العناية الخ) أقول : لا يذهب عنك أن تضعيف العناية لا يصادم
And if there is blood in the wound, and he takes it with a cloth or a fly eats it, and it increases in its place, then if it is such that it would increase and flow if he had not taken it himself, his wudu is invalidated; otherwise not. Similarly, if he throws dust or ash on it, then it appears again, and its dust, then again, then it is all combined. In al-Dhakhira: they said: it is combined only if it is in one sitting, one after another; but if in different sittings, it is not combined. And if he bandaged the wound and moisture penetrated to a layer but not to the outside, it nullifies. In Fath al-Qadir: its meaning must be that were it not for the bandage it would have flowed, because if a shirt rubs against a wound and becomes wet, it does not become impure unless it is like that, because it is not an event. And in al-Muhit: if a tick sucks and becomes full, if it is small, it does not nullify, as if a fly sucked; but if large, it nullifies, like a leech sucking. They reasoned that blood in a large one is flowing. They said: what appears from its place and does not weep, like the blister when peeled, does not nullify; nor what weeps from its place but does not flow, like blood weeping from the socket of a needle, and what is extracted from between teeth, and from bread from biting, and from the finger from inserting it into the nose. And in Munyat al-Musalli: if he clears his nose and blood falls, it does not nullify his wudu; but if a drop of blood drips, it nullifies. As for what flows by squeezing, and were it not for squeezing it would not flow, they said it does not nullify because it is not a mass coming out of his nose; rather it is extracted. This is the choice of the author of al-Hidaya. And Shams al-A'imma said: it nullifies, and it is a deliberate event according to him, and it is the soundest. Like this in Fath al-Qadir attributed to al-Kafi, because extraction or its absence has no apparent effect on this ruling; rather it is due to its being impure effluent, and that is realized with extraction as it is realized without it, so it is like phlebotomy. Moreover, all the evidences from the Sunnah and analogy indicate the suspension of nullification on impure effluent, and that is established in what is extracted. And it is weakened in al-'Inaya because extraction is not textually stipulated, even if it entails it; so its existence is unintentional and not considered. This is all our school, and they supported it with hadiths that al-Fath weakened, and the best evidence is the hadith of Fatimah and analogy. As for the first: what al-Bukhari narrated from 'A'ishah: Fatimah bint Abi Hubaish came to the Prophet (SAW) and said: O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman with istihada and I do not become pure, should I abandon prayer? He said: No, it is only a vein, not menstruation. So then the water of the blister if it penetrates to the bandage, and it is delimited by what al-Fath delimited, so the ruling in it is with flowing and non-flowing. What does not have the force of flow, if it hits the garment, even if in many places, it does not make it impure because what hits the spot does not reach it except as non-flowing moisture, and it is pure, and likewise the rest of the spot. Similarly, if it hits a liquid, it does not make it impure on the sound view. This is a matter of general need and frequent inquiry. Al-Shurunbulali has a treatise on it; it is good to mention its gist. He said after quoting texts: by this you know that the water of a blister that does not flow by its own force is pure, does not nullify wudu, does not impurify the garment or the cloth placed on it, nor the water if it touches it. If it has the force of flowing by itself, then that flowing external is impure and nullifies wudu; it is necessary to wash what of the garment it hits, and the person may not pray while it is flowing because it nullifies wudu and is impure, and he does not become a person with an excuse because he is able to repel his excuse even by bandaging and stuffing that prevents the emission of impurity. And the person with the blister that flows when placed, if he leaves the placement, nothing remains in the spot that flows; so no purification or valid prayer can be imagined at that time because he can prevent by leaving the placement. So he has no way out with placement and flow except by taqlid with observance of conditions in the madhhab he follows, guarding against invalid talfiq. This is the gist of what he mentioned. His saying: (and it is weakened in al-'Inaya): I say: do not be unaware that the weakening of al-'Inaya does not contradict.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage covers several related rulings on wudu nullification by blood and moisture from wounds. First, if blood is wiped away or eaten by flies but still increases in the wound, the test is whether it would have flowed if left alone. If yes, wudu is nullified; otherwise not. When applying dust or ash repeatedly, the total moisture is combined to see if the threshold for flow is reached, but only within one sitting (majlis). Next, if a wound is bandaged and moisture penetrates to the bandage but not outside, it nullifies wudu only if without the bandage it would have flowed. The text also discusses ticks and leeches: a small tick does not nullify, a large one does. Then it lists cases where discharge does not nullify: a peeled blister that does not weep, blood that seeps but does not flow like from a needle prick, particles from teeth, bread, or nose. From Munyat al-Musalli: clearing the nose that results in blood falling does not nullify unless it drips. Finally, a major dispute is presented: if blood is squeezed out and would not have flowed without squeezing, the majority (including Sahib al-Hidayah) say it does not nullify, while Shams al-A'imma says it does, likening it to phlebotomy. The commentary notes that Fath al-Qadir and al-'Inaya weaken the latter view. The best evidence for the Hanafi position is the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaish regarding istihada, which distinguishes between a vein and menstruation. Then a detailed fatwa from al-Shurunbulali on the water of a blister (حمصة): if it flows by itself, it is impure and nullifies wudu; if it does not flow, it is pure and does not nullify. The person is not excused if they can prevent the flow by bandaging.

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-ḥamṣah): A blister or water-filled sac; its water is discussed regarding nullification and impurity.
العصر (al-'aṣr): Squeezing; in context, squeezing blood out of a wound.
التلفيق (al-talfīq): Improper combination of rulings from different madhhabs to avoid prohibition; must be avoided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does the water of a blister that does not flow by its own force nullify wudu?
A) Yes, because it is impure
B) No, because it does not flow
C) Yes, if it touches a garment
D) No, only if the blister is on the face

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi flow test: only naturally flowing impurity from a wound nullifies wudu; non-flowing or extracted blood does not, unless it would have flowed without intervention.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Dispute on Squeezed Blood Nullifying Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute on squeezed blood nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on squeezed blood nullifying wudu and identify which is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Squeezed blood that would not flow naturally does not nullify wudu in the preferred Hanafi view.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement over whether blood that is squeezed out of a wound or pimple nullifies wudu if it would not have flowed without squeezing. The debate centers on whether the impurity of the effluent determines the ruling regardless of extraction, or if extraction must be textually supported to be a nullifier.

Guided Path
1
State the preferred Hanafi position
Identify the view of Sahib al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) that squeezed blood does not nullify wudu because it is not a spontaneous emission.
The author of al-Hidayah is the primary source for the preferred view.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani holds that squeezed blood does nullify wudu as a deliberate event (hadath 'amd).
His view is recorded in al-Kafi and considered the soundest (al-ashah) by some.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand Shams al-A'imma's reasoning: the ruling depends on the impurity of the effluent, not the method of exit; extraction does not change its nature, analogous to phlebotomy.
He sees extraction as irrelevant to the nullifying property of blood.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine al-'Inaya's critique: extraction is not textually stipulated (ghayr mansus 'alayh) and its existence is unintentional (ghayr qasdi) and not considered.
The response emphasizes that only spontaneous flow is supported by primary texts.
5
Determine the thamarah and tarjih
Note the practical consequence: if one squeezes a pimple and blood appears but would not flow naturally, wudu remains valid. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is non-nullification.
Tarjih is based on textual evidence and the principle of not adding to nullifiers without clear proof.

Source Text

وأما ما سال بعصر وكان بحيث لو لم يعصر لم يسل قالوا لا ينقض لأنه ليس بخارج كتلة من أنفه وإنما هو مخرج وهو مختار صاحب الهداية، وقال شمس الأئمة ينقض وهو حدث عمد عنده وهو الأصح كذا في فتح القدير معزياً إلى الكافي لأنه لا تأثير يظهر للإخراج وعدمه في هذا الحكم بل لكونه خارجاً نجساً، وذلك يتحقق مع الاخراج كما يتحقق مع عدمه فصار كالفصد، كيف وجميع الأدلة الموردة من السنة والقياس يفيد تعليق النقض بالخارج النجس وهو ثابت في المخرج اهـ. وضعفه في العناية بأن الإخراج ليس بمنصوص عليه وإن كان يستلزمه فكان ثبوته غير قصدي ولا معتبر به ا هـ. وهذا كله مذهبنا واستدلوا له بأحاديث ضعفها في فتح القدير وأحسن ما يستدل به حديث فاطمة والقياس. أما الأول فما رواه البخاري عن عائشة جاءت فاطمة بنت أبي حبيش إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت : يا رسول الله إني امرأة استحاض فلا أطهر أفأدع الصلاة؟ قال : لا إنما ذلك عرق وليست بالحيضة، فإذا
As for what flows by squeezing, and were it not for squeezing it would not flow, they said it does not nullify because it is not a mass coming out of his nose; rather it is extracted. This is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah. And Shams al-A'imma said: it nullifies, and it is a deliberate event according to him, and it is the soundest. Like this in Fath al-Qadir attributed to al-Kafi, because extraction or its absence has no apparent effect on this ruling; rather it is due to its being impure effluent, and that is realized with extraction as it is realized without it, so it is like phlebotomy. Moreover, all the evidences from the Sunnah and analogy indicate the suspension of nullification on impure effluent, and that is established in what is extracted. And it is weakened in al-'Inaya because extraction is not textually stipulated, even if it entails it; so its existence is unintentional and not considered. This is all our school, and they supported it with hadiths that al-Fath weakened, and the best evidence is the hadith of Fatimah and analogy. As for the first: what al-Bukhari narrated from 'A'ishah: Fatimah bint Abi Hubaish came to the Prophet (SAW) and said: O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman with istihada and I do not become pure, should I abandon prayer? He said: No, it is only a vein, not menstruation. So when...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether blood that is squeezed out of a wound or nose nullifies wudu if it would not have flowed without squeezing. The author of al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) chooses the view that it does not nullify, reasoning that it is not a spontaneous emission but rather a forced extraction, and thus not a 'mass' coming out. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, however, holds that it does nullify because the ruling is based on the effluent being impure, regardless of whether it was extracted or flowed spontaneously; he analogizes it to phlebotomy (فصد). The commentary notes that Fath al-Qadir attributes the latter view to al-Kafi and calls it the soundest (الأصح). However, al-'Inaya weakens this view, arguing that extraction is not explicitly mentioned in the primary texts and is unintentional, thus not a valid basis for nullification. The passage concludes by stating that the Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaish and analogy (qiyas) as evidence for its position. The hadith distinguishes between menstrual blood and a vein, implying that not all blood nullifies ritual purity. The text is cut off but continues the discussion.

Key Terms
العصر (al-'aṣr): Squeezing; in this context, squeezing a wound to extract blood.
الفصد (al-faṣd): Phlebotomy; intentionally drawing blood for medical purposes, which nullifies wudu by analogy.
الحدث العمد (al-ḥadath al-'amd): Deliberate event; Shams al-A'imma considered squeezed blood a deliberate nullifier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It nullifies only if done intentionally

When Blood From Wounds and Bumps Nullifies Wudu
Stating when blood from wounds and bumps nullifies wudu and causes impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions under which blood from wounds and bumps nullifies wudu and makes objects impure, and distinguish between flowing and non-flowing blood based on the criterion of sayalan.

Core Idea
Only flowing blood nullifies wudu and is impure; non-flowing blood is pure and does not break wudu.

This lesson examines a common practical question: when does blood from a wound or pimple break wudu and make objects impure? The Hanafi school provides a clear criterion—the force of flow (sayalan). Drawing from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and the treatise of al-Shurunbulali, we will explore the specific rulings for wounds, bumps, and related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key issue
The ruling concerns whether blood exiting the body nullifies wudu and is considered impure. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the blood flows.
2
Define the flow criterion (sayalan)
Flow is defined as the blood moving by its own force, not merely appearing or being transferred by touch. Understand the difference between flowing and non-flowing.
Non-flowing includes blood that remains on the skin, in a scab, or residue between teeth.
3
Apply to examples from the text
Work through the examples: bandaged wound, scab, needle prick, nose sniff, bump fluid. For each, decide if the blood flows and thus nullifies.
The passage emphasizes that if blood would flow were the bandage removed, it is flowing.
4
Recognize the practical impact
Non-flowing blood does not require washing garments or renewing wudu. This eases daily practice for minor wounds and pimples.
Al-Shurunbulali's treatise confirms this is a widespread issue with clear guidance.
5
Understand the case of a chronic condition
For a bump that oozes only when pressed, the person can prevent flow by not pressing, so he must do so to maintain wudu. If unable, taqlid may be considered.
This illustrates the principle that one must avoid the nullifier if possible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Such that without the bandage it would flow, because if the shirt is placed over the wound and becomes wet, it does not become impure unless it is like that because it is not an event. In al-Muhit: sucking a tick that becomes full; if it is small, it does not nullify like sucking a fly; if it is large, it nullifies like sucking a leech. They explained that the blood in a large one is flowing. They said: What appears from its place and does not rise, like the scab when peeled, does not nullify; nor what rises from its place but does not flow, like blood rising from the needle's puncture, and the residue between teeth, and in bread from biting, and in the finger from inserting it into the nose. In Munyat al-Musalli: If he sniffs and blood falls, it does not nullify his wudu, but if a drop of blood drips, it does nullify. As for the bump: if it penetrates the bandage, and it is restricted to what al-Fath restricts it to, the ruling for it is with flowing and without. What does not have the force of flow, if it touches the garment even in many places, it does not make it impure because the affected place does not reach from it except non-flowing moisture which is pure, and similarly for the rest of the place. Similarly, if it touches a liquid, it does not make it impure according to the correct view. This is a widespread issue with many questions, and al-Shurunbulali has a treatise on it; it is good to mention its summary. He said after narrating the transmissions: from this you know that the water of the bump that does not flow by its own force is pure, it does not nullify wudu, nor make impure the garment, nor the cloth placed on it, nor the water if it touches it. If it has the force of flow by itself, then that flowing liquid is impure, nullifying wudu, and it is necessary to wash what it contacts from the garment, and the person may not pray during its flow because it is impure and nullifies wudu, and he does not become a person with an excuse because he can prevent it by bandaging and packing. The person with the bump that flows when placed, if he refrains from placing it, nothing remains to flow, so he cannot imagine purity or valid prayer then because he can prevent it by not placing it. So there is no escape for him with placement and flow except by taqlid with observance of conditions in the school he follows to avoid corrupt talfiq. This is the summary of what he mentioned.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining when blood from wounds and bumps nullifies wudu and causes impurity. It begins by discussing a wound covered with a bandage: if blood soaks through but would not flow without the bandage, it does not nullify wudu or make the shirt impure because it is not considered an 'event' (hadath). The key is whether the blood flows freely. Similarly, sucking a small tick does not nullify wudu, but a large one does because the blood in a large tick is flowing. The passage then lists several cases where blood appears but does not flow: a scab when peeled, blood from a needle prick, residue between teeth, from biting bread, or from inserting a finger into the nose—all do not nullify wudu. From Munyat al-Musalli: sniffing and having blood fall without dripping does not nullify, but if a drop drips, it does. The second part focuses on a bump (hamsa): if the fluid from it does not have the force to flow by itself, it is pure and does not nullify wudu or make objects impure. If it flows by itself, it is impure and nullifies wudu, and the person must wash affected areas and cannot pray while it flows unless he has an excuse (like inability to stop it). The person with a bump that flows only when pressed can prevent it by not pressing, so he cannot validly pray without stopping it. The only solution for such a person is to follow another madhhab (taqlid) with proper conditions to avoid talfiq. This is the Hanafi position as summarized by al-Shurunbulali.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): Legal event that nullifies wudu (minor impurity).
حمصة (hamsah): A bump, nodule, or pimple that may ooze fluid.
سيلان (sayalan): Flowing; the force of flow by which liquid moves independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fluid is impure and nullifies wudu.
B) The fluid is pure and does not nullify wudu.
C) The fluid is impure but does not nullify wudu.
D) The fluid is pure but nullifies wudu.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the nullification of wudu and impurity of blood depend on the force of flow; non-flowing blood remains pure and does not break wudu.

Blood Exiting by Squeezing: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on blood exit by squeezing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on blood exiting by squeezing and evaluate the tarjih between them.

Core Idea
Intentional squeezing of a wound: does the blood nullify wudu? The Hanafi school is divided.

This passage explores an internal dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the nullification of wudu by blood that exits due to squeezing. The disagreement centers on whether the effective cause (illa) is natural flow or impure exit itself. Understanding this dispute illuminates the method of ta'lil (rationing) and the role of ihtiyat (caution) versus leniency in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two conflicting positions
Determine what action is being discussed (squeezing a wound) and the two rulings: does it nullify wudu or not?
The first position (al-Hidayah) says no; the second (Shams al-A'immah) says yes.
2
Extract the proof for the first position
Find why al-Hidayah and others hold that squeezing does not nullify. They argue that natural flow is the cause, and squeezing produces removal, not natural exit.
They also distinguish squeezing from cupping, where skin is cut.
3
Extract the proof for the second position
Find why Shams al-A'immah and others hold nullification. Their key point: impure exit is the cause, which is present whether natural or by removal.
They analogize to cupping and phlebotomy.
4
Analyze the rebuttals and counter-rebuttals
Read al-‘Inayah's critique (removal not textually stipulated) and the response from al-Athqani (exit necessarily follows removal). Understand the nuanced arguments.
Also note the analogy of the waterskin.
5
Determine the tarjih and its practical consequence
Identify which view is considered more correct (as-sahh) and why. Know the thamarah al-khilaf: if blood is squeezed out, wudu is either void or intact depending on the view followed.
The mufta bihi is the view of Shams al-A'immah (nullification).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما سال بعصر وكان بحيث لو لم يعصر لم يسل قالوا لا ينقض لأنه ليس بخارج كتلة من أنفه وإنما هو مخرج وهو مختار صاحب الهداية، وقال شمس الأئمة ينقض وهو حدث عمد عنده وهو الأصح كذا في فتح القدير معزياً إلى الكافي لأنه لا تأثير يظهر للإخراج وعدمه في هذا الحكم بل لكونه خارجاً نجساً، وذلك يتحقق مع الاخراج كما يتحقق مع عدمه فصار كالفصد، كيف وجميع الأدلة الموردة من السنة والقياس يفيد تعليق النقض بالخارج النجس وهو ثابت في المخرج اهـ. وضعفه في العناية بأن الإخراج ليس بمنصوص عليه وإن كان يستلزمه فكان ثبوته غير قصدي ولا معتبر به ا هـ. قوله : (وضعفه في العناية الخ) أقول : لا يذهب عنك أن تضعيف العناية لا يصادم البحر الرائق ج ١/ م ٥ قول شمس الأئمة وهو الأصح ، وفي حاشية أخي زاده على صدر الشريعة قوله : إذا عصر القرحة قيل عدم النقص ههنا على اختيار الظهيرية والهداية، وذهب صاحب التتمة والخلاصة والكافي والسرخسي إلى أن المخرج ناقض كالخارج قياساً على الحجامة والفصد ومص العلقة. وقال الأتقاني : لأن الاحتياط فيه وإن كان الرفق بالناس في الأوّل، وتحقيقه عندي : أن الخروج لازم الإخراج ولا بد من وجود اللازم عند وجود الملزوم فيحصل الناقض حينئذ لا محالة فافهم .اهـ. كلامه. وأما وجه القول الأول فلأن علة النقض هي الخروج بالطبع والسيلان وقد انتفى والقياس على المذكورات غير مستقيم لأن في كل منها يخرج الدم بعد قطع الجلدة فهو بمنزلة ارتفاع المانع حتى صرحوا بأن المص إذا كان بحيث لا يسيل الدم بعد سقوط العلقة لا ينقض، وما نحن فيه ليس كذلك لأن علة الخروج هي العصر فإنه يشبه شق زق الغير ثم عصره والمص يشبه شقه ثم تركه فإنه يضمن في الأول دون الثاني .اهـ. وإذا تأملت لم يعجزك رد ما أتى به فتأمل. قاله الرملي . أقول : أي لم يعجزك رد ما وجه به أخي زاده القول الأول وكان مراده به منع قوله إن علة النقض
As for what flows by squeezing and is such that if not squeezed it would not flow, they said it does not nullify because it is not exiting of its own accord but is removed, and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah. Shams al-A'immah said it nullifies, and it is an intentional event in his view, and it is the most correct, as in Fath al-Qadeer attributed to al-Kafi, because removal or non-removal has no apparent effect in this ruling, rather it is due to it being an impure exit, and that is realized with removal as it is realized without it, so it becomes like cupping. How can it be that all the proofs from sunnah and qiyas indicate that nullification depends on impure exit, and that is established in the removed thing? He weakened this in al-Inayah by saying that removal is not textually stipulated, and even if it is necessary, its existence is unintentional and not considered. He said: (and its weakening in al-Inayah, etc.) I say: it should not escape you that the weakening in al-Inayah does not contradict al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 1, p. 5. The statement of Shams al-A'immah is the most correct. In the marginalia of Akhi Zada on Sadr al-Shari'ah, he said: When he squeezes a sore, some say no nullification here according to the choice of al-Dhahiriyya and al-Hidayah; while the author of al-Tatimma, al-Khulasa, al-Kafi, and al-Sarakhsi held that the removed thing nullifies like the exiting, by analogy to cupping, phlebotomy, and sucking a leech. Al-Athqani said: Because caution is in it, though leniency for people is in the first. And my verification is that exit is necessary for removal, and the necessary must exist when the necessary condition exists, so the nullifier inevitably occurs then, so understand. As for the face of the first opinion, it is because the cause of nullification is natural exit and flow, and that is absent, and analogy to the mentioned things is not correct because in each of those blood exits after cutting the skin, so it is like removal of a barrier, and they explicitly stated that sucking if the blood does not flow after the leech falls does not nullify. Our case is not like that because the cause of exit is squeezing, which resembles slitting another's waterskin then squeezing it, while sucking resembles slitting it then leaving it; in the first case he guarantees, in the second not. And if you reflect, you will not be unable to refute what he brought. Al-Ramli said: I say: you will not be unable to refute what Akhi Zada adduced for the first opinion, and his intent was to prevent his statement that the cause of nullification
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding blood that flows from a wound or sore due to intentional squeezing. The issue is whether such blood nullifies wudu. The author of al-Hidayah and some others hold that it does not nullify because the blood is not naturally exiting but is being 'removed' (mukhrij). Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani, however, argues that it does nullify, and his view is considered the more correct (as-sahh) in works like Fath al-Qadeer. The reasoning is that the impure exit is the effective cause, and that exists whether the blood exits naturally or by removal. Responding to this, al-‘Inayah argues that removal is not explicitly mentioned in the primary texts, and even if it necessarily leads to exit, the removal itself is not legally intended. The commentator then quotes Akhi Zada's marginalia, which lists scholars on both sides: some (like Sahib al-Hidayah and al-Dhahiriyya) say no nullification; others (like al-Sarakhsi, al-Kafi, etc.) say it does nullify by analogy to cupping. Al-Athqani prefers caution and argues that since exit necessarily follows removal, nullification occurs. The first opinion bases nullification on natural flow, which is absent here, and distinguishes squeezing from cupping because in cupping the skin is cut, so blood exits naturally, while squeezing is an additional act. A further analogy is given: squeezing a wound is like someone else's waterskin that is slit and then squeezed (guarantee applies), while sucking is like slitting it and leaving it (no guarantee). The commentator concludes that the reader can refute Akhi Zada's argument. This dispute shows the rich internal debate within the Hanafi school on the effective cause of nullification.

Key Terms
عصر (asr): Squeezing; applying pressure to cause fluid to exit.
مخرج (mukhrij): Removed; blood that exits due to an external action like squeezing.
فصد (fasd): Phlebotomy; opening a vein to let blood.
الخارج النجس (al-kharij al-najis): Impure exit; the substance that exits and is considered impure (najis).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shams al-A'immah, why does blood that flows due to squeezing nullify wudu?
A) Because squeezing is intentional and thus a greater impurity.
B) Because impure exit is the cause, and it is present whether the exit is natural or by removal.
C) Because the person intended to nullify wudu.
D) Because the blood is more impure when squeezed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school internally disagrees on whether blood that exits by intentional squeezing nullifies wudu, but the relied-upon view is that it does nullify, based on the illa of impure exit.
